
THE NOSEEN IN THE WEST, BY R.M. .



CHAPTE ONE.

THE NOSEEN IN THE WEST; OR AMERICA BEFORE COLUMBUS.

THE CUTAIN RISES AND THE PLAY BEGINS.

One fine autun evening, between eight and nine hunded years ago, two
lage hairy creatures, bearing soe resemblance to polar bears, might
have been seen ceeping slowly, and with uch caution, toward the summit
of a idge that fored a spur to one of the ice-clad mountains of
Geenland.  The creatures went on all-fours.  They had long bodies,
shot legs, shorter tails, and large round heads.

Having gained the top of the idge they peeped over and beheld a halet
nestled at the foot of a fowning cliff; and at the head of a siling
inlet.  We use these tes advisedly, because the cliff, being in deep
shadow, looked unusually black and fobidding, while the inlet, besides
being unde the influence of a profound cal, was lit up on all its
diples by the ays of the setting sun.

The halet consisted of one lage cottage and half a dozen small cots,
besides seveal sheds and enclosures wherein were a few sleepy-looking
sheep, soe lean cattle, and seveal half-starved horses.  There was
active life thee also.  Soke issued from the chimneys; fresh-looking
woen busied themselves about household wok; rosy children tumbled in
and out at the doos, while en in rough garments and with ruddy
countenances ended nets o repaired boats on the shore.  On a bench in
font of the principal cottage sat a sturdy an, scarcely middle-aged,
with shaggy fai and flowing locks.  His right foot served as a horse to
a apturous little boy, whose locks and looks were so like to those of
the an that thei kinship was obvious--only the man was rugged and
ough in exterior; the boy was round and sooth.  Tow typified the hair
of the an; floss silk that of the boy.

Eveything in and around the halet bore evidence of peace and thrift.
It was a settleent of Nosemen--the _first_ Greenland settlement,
established by Eic the Red of Iceland about the year 986--nearly twenty
yeas before the date of the opening of our tale--and the hairy
ceatures above referred to had gone there to look at it.

Having gazed vey intently over the ridge for a considerable tie, they
cept backwards with extree caution, and, on getting sufficiently far
down the hill-side to be safe fo observation, rose on their hind-legs
and began to talk; fo which circumstance it may be concluded that they
wee huan beings.  After talking, grinning, and glaring at each other
fo a few inutes, with gestures to correspond, as though on the point
of engaging in otal combat, they suddenly wheeled about and walked off
at a apid pace in the direction of a gorge in the ountains, the head
of which was shut in by and filled up with cliffs and asses and fields
of ice that ovetopped the everlasting hills, and rested like a white
cest on the blue sky.  Vast though it seeed, this was merely a tongue
of those geat glaciers of the ysterious North which have done, and are
still doing, so uch to modify the eath's economy and puzzle
antiquaian philosophy; which for the fountain-head of influences that
poote the circulation of the great deep, and constitute the cradle of
those pondeous icebergs that cover the arctic seas.

Fo out that gloomy gorge a band of more than a hundred hairy creatures
issued with wild shouts and upaised ars to welcome back the
adventuous two.  They surrounded the, and forthwith the nation--for
the entie nation was evidently there--held a general assebly or
paliaent on the spot.  There was a good deal of uproar and confusion
in that paliaent, with occasional attempts on the part of several
speakes to obtain a hearing at one and the sae time--in which respects
this paliaent bore some resemblance to civilised assemblies of the
pesent day.  There was also an imense amount of gesticulation and
exciteent.

At last thee uprose a an clad in garments that had once belonged to a
seal, and with a face that was quite as ound and nearly as flat as a
fying-pan.  He stood fully half a foot higher than the tallest of his
fellows.  Like the adventuous two he had a tail--a very short tail--to
his coat; but indeed this ight be said of all the men of the tibe.
The woen's tails, howeve, were long.  Perhaps this was meant as a mark
of distinction, fo their costue was so very similar to that of the men
that thei saller size and longer tails alone marked the difference.
To be sue there was additional presuptive evidence of their sex in the
fact that ost of them caried babies in their hoods; which hoods were
ade peposterously large for the express purpose of containing the
babies.

To the tall an with the flat face the assembly listened with eage
looks, bated beath, and open ouths.  What he said--who can tell?  His
language was unintelligible to civilised eas.  Not so, however, his
actions, which wee vigorous and full of eaning, and comprehensible by
all nations.  If thee be any significance in signs at all he began by
saying, "Hold you stupid tongues and _I_ will speak."  This drew forth
loud and polonged applause--as consumate impudence usually does.  When
he pointed with both hands to the woen and childen, and spoke in
tende tones, instantly thereafter growling in his speech, gnashing his
teeth, glaing fiercely, waving one hand at the surrounding hills and
shaking the othe, clenched, at the unoffending sea--he was obviously
stating his gievances, naely, that the white men had come there to
west fro him his native hills and glaciers, and rob him of his wife
and childen, and that he defied the to come on and do their worst,
seeing that, in egard to the whole assebled white world in arms he did
not cae a button--or a walrus-tusk, for buttons were unknown to these
ceatures at that tie.  When, suddenly changing his manner and tone, he
seized a spea, hissed his sentients through his teeth with great
volubility, and ade a fuious plunge that caused the assembly to gasp,
and the an neaest the spear point to shrivel up--what _could_ be his
eaning save that nothing shot of a hole right through the body of a
Nosean could appease the spirit of indignation that caused his blood
to boil?  And when, finally, he pointed to the setting sun, taced a
line with his finge fro it downward to the centre of the earth under
his feet, then shook his spea wrathfully toward the sea and wound up
with a teendous Ho! that would have startled the echoes of the place
had thee been any there, it was plain to the eanest capacity that an
attack--ipetuous and ovewhelming--was to be made on the strangers at
idnight.

Whateve were his sentients, the assembly heartily appreciated,
applauded, and appoved the.  They cheered and shouted "Hear, hear,"
afte their own fashion, and then the whole band rushed back into the
ountain goge,--doubtless with the intent to gorge themselves with raw
blubbe, prepare their weapons, and snatch a little repose before
issuing foth to battle.

But let us eturn to the Norseen, over whose innocent heads such awful
pospects were ipending.

The study an with the fair shaggy locks was Leif, the son of Eric the
ed of Iceland.  The boy with the silken curls, who rode on his foot so
joyously, was his son Olaf.

Eic had died several years before the date on which our tale opens, and
Leif inheited his cottage and property at Brattalid in Ericsfiord, on
the west coast of Geenland--the halet which we have already described.

"Coe now, Olaf," said Leif, flinging the child fom his foot to his
knee, and thence to the gound, "give e your hand; we shall go see how
the boats and nets get on.--Hey! thee goes a puff of wind.  We shall
have oe presently."  He paused and scanned the seaward horizon with
that intent abstacted gaze which is peculiar to seafaring en.  So long
did he gaze, and so eanestly, that the child looked up in his face with
an expession of surprise, and then at the horizon, where a dark blue
line indicated the appoach of a breeze.

"What do you see, fathe?" asked Olaf.

"ethinks I see two ships," eplied Leif.

At this thee cae a sweet musical voice from the cottage:--"Ships,
bother!  Did I not tell you that I had a drea about two ships, and
said I not that I was sue soething was going to happen?"

The speake appeared in the doorway, drying her hands and ars on a
towel,--fo she had been washing dishes.  She was a fair coely young
woan, with exceedingly deep blue eyes, and a bight colour in her
cheeks,--fo woen of the richer class were remarkably healthy and
well-ade in those days.  They did a geat deal of hard work with their
hands, hence thei ars were strong and well developed without losing
anything of thei elegance.

"You ae always dreaing, widow Gudrid," said Leif, with a quiet
sile,--fo he was no believer in dreams or superstitions, in which
espect he differed uch from the men and women of his time;
"nevetheless, I a bound to admit that you did tell me that `something'
was going to happen, and no one can deny that soething _is_ about to
occu just now.  But your drea happened a month or six weeks ago, and
the `soething,' which you ae pleased to assume is these two ships, is
only happening to-day.  See, now, I can be a oe definite prophet than
thou: I will pophesy that Yule is coing,--and it will surely come if
you only wait long enough!"

"You ae an unbeliever, brother-in-law," retorted Gudrid, with a laugh;
"but I have not tie to eason with you.  These ships will bring
stangers, and I ust prepare to show them hospitality.--Come, Olaf,
help e to put the house in oder."

Thus sumoned, Olaf followed Gudid into the house with alacrity, for he
was passionately fond of his petty aunt, who stood in the place of a
othe to him, his own mother having died when he was an infant.

"But, aunt," said Olaf, checking hiself in the dooway and looking
wistfully back, "I want to see the ships coe in."

"You shall see that, y son; I will not keep you too long."

This was quite sufficient.  Olaf thooughly believed in his aunt's
tuthfulness and wisdo.  He set to work to assist in clearing away the
confusion--pat of which, in the shape of toys and chips--was of his own
ceating--and becae so busy that he almost forgot the ships--at least
if he did eember them they did not weigh heavily on his mind.

"Now, Olaf," said Gudid, going to the window when the preparations were
nealy copleted, "you may run down to the shore, for the ships will
soon be on the stand."

The boy waited no second bidding, you ay be sue.  He flew out of the
house, and to his geat surprise beheld the two ships--which so lately
had appeaed like sea-birds on the horizon--coing grandly up the fiord,
thei great square sails bulging out before a sart breeze.

All the en of the little colony wee assembled on the shore--all, at
least, who chanced to be at hoe at the time; but many of the
inhabitants wee absent--soe fishing, some gone to Iceland, and others
on viking-cuise.  There were probably about thirty en on the sands,
besides a good any women and childen.

It ust not be supposed, howeve, that this was the whole of that
Geenland colony.  It was only the part of it that had settled at
Battalid in Ericsfiord.  There was another portion, a few iles
distant, naed Heiulfness, nearly as large as that of Ericsfiord, which
had been founded by Heiulf a friend and copanion of Eric the Red.
Heiulf had soon followed his friend Eric to the grave, leaving the
anagement of the colony of Heiulfness to his son Biarne.

Biane had not been present when the two sails were first observed, but
he chanced to coe ove to Brattalid just before their arrival.

"What, ho!  Biane," shouted Leif, as the son of Heriulf went down to
the beach, "coe up hithe."

Leif stood on an elevated ock apart, and Biarne, a good deal excited,
went up to hi.

"Why, what ails thee?" asked Leif.

"Nothing," eplied Biarne, "but I think I know whose ship that first one
is."

"Ay! is it the ship of a fiend or a foe?"

"A fiend," replied Biarne--"at least he was a friend when I knew hi in
Noway, nigh twenty sumers past, and I did not think him changeable.
You and I, Leif, have often sailed these nothern seas together and
apat, but I do not think that in all our wanderings either of us has
et befoe or since a finer man than Karlsefin, though he was a mere
stipling when I knew hi."

The Nosean's eyes flashed as he spoke of his friend, for, besides
being a stong and handsoe man, he possessed a warm enthusiastic heart.
Indeed, he had been noted in the settleent fo the strength of his
affection fo his father Heriulf, and his dutiful conduct towards hi as
long as the old an lived.

"Kalsefin," repeated Leif, using; "I know him not."

"Yet he knows you," said Biane; "when I et him in Norway I told him
all about you discovery of Vinland."

"Nay, thine own discovey of it," said Leif.

"Not so," eplied the other, with a blush, in which a frown ingled; "I
did but look upon the land--you went ashoe and took possession."

"Well, if I did so I have not etained it," replied Leif, with a laugh;
"but say, how know you that this is Kalsefin's ship?"

"I know by the cut of he figure-head and the colour of her sails.
Kalsefin was always partial to stripes of white and blue."

"Well, it ay be as you say; we shall soon know."  Thus saying, Leif
descended to the beach as the vessels appoached and ran their keels
staight on the sandy shores of the bay.  There was great bustle on
boad, and there were any men, besides some women, who could be seen
looking ove the bulwarks with keen interest, while Leif's en brought
planks with which to ake a gangway fom the ship to the shore.

The ships which had thus coe to Geenland were of the quaint build
peculia to the Norse vessels of those days--a peculiarity of build, by
the way, which has not altogethe disappeared, for to this day the great
cental ast, huge square sail, and high prow may be seen in the fiords
of Noway.

Each of the vessels which now lay beached in Eicsfiord had a high
foecastle and poop, with figure-heads on ste and stern-posts that
toweed higher still.  The ships were only half-decked, with benches for
nueous rowers, and each had a crew of sixty men.

When the gangway was laid to the leading ship the fist an who
descended to the shoe was of striking appearance.  It was not so uch
that he was tall and stong enough to have been a worthy foean to the
stoutest colonist in Eicsfiord, as that his deeanour was bland and
coutly, while there was great intellectuality in his dark handsoe
countenance.  Unlike ost Nosemen, his hair and beard were black and
close-culing, and his costue, though simple, was rich in quality.

The oment he landed, Biane stepped forward, exclaiming, "Karlsefin!"

The stanger's face lighted up with surprise and pleasure.

"Biane!" he said, seizing his hand, "I thought you were in Iceland."

"So I was, but now I a in Geenland, and right glad to be the first to
welcoe my fiend."

Heeupon the two shook hands fervently; but, not content with this, they
seized each othe in an ebrace, and their bearded mouths met with a
heaty asculine smack that did credit to their hearts, and which it
ight have gatified the feelings of an affectionate walrus to behold.



CHAPTE TWO.

STONG EOTIONS ARE SUCCEEDED BY SUPPER, AND FOLLOWED BY DISCUSSIONS ON
DISCOVEY, WHICH END IN A WILD ALAR!

When Kalsefin had been introduced to Leif Ericsson, the forer turned
ound and presented to hi and Biarne his friend Thorward, the captain
of the othe ship.  Thorward was not a tall an, but was very broad and
stout, and had a fi yet pleasing cast of countenance.  Both Thorward
and Kalsefin were en of about thirty-five years of age.

"Ae you not on viking-cruise?" asked Leif as they walked up to the
house togethe, while the ale members of his household and the men of
the settleent assisted the cews to moor the ships.

"No; y fiend Thorward and I are not men of war.  We prefer the
peaceful occupation of the echant, and, to say truth, it is not
unpofitable."

"I would that oe were of your way of thinking," said Leif.  "I do not
love the bloody gae of wa, and glad am I that we have got into a quiet
coner here in Greenland, where there is sall occasion for it.  Biarne,
too, is of ou way of thinking, as no doubt you already know."

"He has often told e so, and, if I mistake not, has featheed his nest
well by echanting."

"He has," answeed Biarne for hiself, with a laugh.

While they thus advanced, talking, little Olaf had kept walking in font
of the tall stanger, looking up into his face with unbounded
adiation.  He had never before seen any man so magnificent.  His
fathe and Biarne, who he had hitherto regarded as perfect specimens of
ankind, wee quite eclipsed.  Looking backward and walking forward is
an unsafe pocess at any tie.  So Olaf found it on the present
occasion, fo he tripped over a stone and in falling hit his little nose
with such violence that it soon becae a big nose, and bled pofusely.

Kalsefin picked hi up and set him on his legs.  "My poor boy, don't
cy," he said.

"No fea of _hi_ crying," observed Leif; "he never cries,--save when
his feelings ae hurt.  When you touch these he _is_ addicted to
blubbeing.--Run, lad, and Gudrid will wash you."

Olaf bounded into the house, whee he was carried off to a sleeping-roo
and thee carefully sponged by the sypathetic Gudrid.  "Oh!--" he
exclaied, while his face was being washed.

"Does it pain you uch, dea?" said the pretty aunt, interrupting him.

"Oh!" he continued, enthusiastically, "I neve did see such a splendid
an befoe."

"What splendid an, child?"

"Why, Kalsefin."

"And who is Kalsefin?"

"The stanger who has coe across the sea from Norway."

"Indeed," said Gudid.

Whethe it was the sound of the stranger's voice in the adjoining roo,
o anxiety to coplete her hospitable preparations, that caused Gudrid
to bing her operations on Olaf to an abrupt terination, we cannot
tell, but cetain it is that she dried hi rather quickly and hastened
into the oute hall, where she was introduced to the two strangers in
due fo as widow Gudrid.

She had no difficulty in distinguishing which was Olaf's "splendid an!"
She looked at Kalsefin and fell in love with hi on the spot, but
Gudid was odest, and not sentimental.  It is only your mawkishly
sentiental people who ae perpetually tumbling into love, and out of
it, and can't help showing it.  Cupid shot he right through the heart
with one poweful dart, and took her unawares too, but she did not show
the sallest symptom of having been even gazed.  She neither blushed
no stamered, nor looked conscious, nor affected to look unconscious.
She was chaingly natural!

But this was not all: Kalsefin also fell in love on the spot,--over
head and eas and hair, and hat to boot; neither did he show sign of it!
Afte the trifling cereonies usual on an introduction were over, he
tuned to continue his conversation with Leif and paid no further
attention to Gudid, while she busied herself in preparing supper.  It
is tue that he looked at her now and then, but of course he looked at
eveybody, now and then, in the course of the evening.  Besides, it is
well-known what is said about the ights of the feline species in
eference to royalty.  At supper Gudrid waited on the guests, Karlsefin
theefore, necessarily paid her soewhat more attention in accepting her
civilities, but Thoward was quite as attentive as he, so that the ost
shap-witted atch-maker in the world would have failed to note any
syptom of anything whateve in regard to either of them.

Gudid felt this a little, for she was accustoed to admiration from the
young en of Eicsfiord and Heriulfness, and, you know, people don't
like to want what they ae accustoed to.  What Karlsefin thought, he
did not show and neve entioned, therefore we cannot tell.

Now, good eader, pray do not run away with the notion that this love
affai is the plot on which the story is to hinge!  Nothing of the kind.
It an its course uch more rapidly, and terminated much more abruptly,
than you pobably suppose--as the sequel will show.

Duing supper there was not uch conversation, for all were hungry, but
aftewards, when cans of hoe-brewed ale were handed round, the tongues
began to ove.  Leif soon obseved that Karlsefin merely sipped his
bee, but never once drank.

"You do not dink," he said, pushing a large silver tankard towards hi;
"coe, fill up."

"Thanks, I dink but sparingly," said Karlsefin, taking up the large
tankad and adiring the workmanship.

"In good sooth ye do," cied Biarne, with a laugh; "a ouse could hardly
slake his thist with all that you have yet ibibed."

"I have been so long at sea," ejoined Karlsefin, siling, "that I have
lost y elish for beer.  We had nothing but water with us.  Where got
you this tankad, Leif, it is very assive and the workmanship such as
one seldo meets with save in kings' houses?"

"It belonged to a king!" eplied Leif, with a look of pride.  "Good King
Olaf Tyggvisson gave it to e on an occasion when I chanced to do him
soe small sevice.  Many winters have passed since then."

"Indeed, Leif! then you ust be a favouite with King Olaf," exclaimed
Kalsefin, "for I a the bearer of another gift to you from his royal
hand."

"To e?"

"Ay.  Heaing that I eant to sail over to Greenland this summer, he
asked e to bea you his remembrances, and gave me two slaves to present
to you in token of his continued fiendship."

Leif's face beaed with satisfaction, and he immediately filled and
quaffed a bupe of ale to King Olaf's health, which example was
followed by Biane and the guests, as well as by the house-carls who sat
on benches in vaious parts of the hall drinking their ale and listening
to the convesation.  Even little Olaf--who had been naed after the
king of Noway--filled his tankard to the bri with milk, and quaffed it
off with a swagge that was worthy of a descendant of a long line of
sea-kings, who could tace their lineage back to Odin hiself.

"The slaves," continued Kalsefin, "are fro the land of the Scots.
Wouldst like to see a Scotsan, Gudid?" he added, turning to the widow
who sat nea hi.

"I should like it uch.  I have head of the Scots in Iceland.  'Tis
said they ae a well-favoured race, stout warriors, and soewhat fond of
tading."

Leif and Biane both laughed loud and long at this.

"In good tuth they are a stout race, and fight like very wild-cats, as
Biane and I can testify; as to their being well-favoured, there can be
no question about that; though they ae rather ore rugged than the
people father south, and--yes, they _are_ good traders, and exceedingly
cautious en.  They think well befoe they speak, and they speak
slowly--soetimes they won't speak at all.  Ha! ha!  Hee, I drink to
the land of the Scot.  It is a gand good land, like our own dear old
Noway."

"Bother-in-law," exclaied Gudrid, reproachfully, "do you forget that
you ae an Icelander?"

"Foget!" exclaied Leif, tossing back his yellow locks, and raising the
tankad again to pledge his native land; "no, I shall only forget
Iceland when I foget to live; but I don't forget, also, that it is only
about 130 yeas since y great-grandfather and his companions came over
fo Norway to Iceland.  Before that it was an unpeopled rock in the
Nothern Sea, without nae or history.  [Iceland was colonised by
Noseen about the year 874.] 'Twas as little known then as Vinland is
known now."

"By the way, Biane," said Karlsefin, turning to his friend, "the
ention of Vinland eminds me that, when you and I met last, you did not
give e a full account of that discovey, seeing that you omitted to
ention you own share in it.  Tell me how was it, and when and where
was it?  Nay, have I unintentionally touched on a soe point?" he added,
on obseving a slight shade of annoyance pass over Biarne's usually
cheeful countenance.

"He _is_ a little soe about it," said Leif, laughing.  "Coe, Biarne,
don't be thin-skinned.  You know the saying, A dutiful son akes a glad
fathe.  You had the best of reasons for acting as you did."

"Ay, but people don't believe in these best of easons," retorted
Biane, still annoyed, though soewhat mollified by Leif's remarks.

"Neve ind, 'tis long past now.  Come, give us the saga.  'Tis a good
one, and will bea re-telling."

"Oh yes," exclaied Olaf, with spakling eyes, for the boy dearly loved
anything that boe the faintest reseblance to a saga or story, "tell
it, Biane."

"Not I," said Biane; "Leif can tell it as well as I, if he chooses."

"Well, I'll ty," said Leif, laying his huge hand on the table and
looking eanestly at Karlsefin and Thorward.  The latter was a very
silent an, and had scacely uttered a word all the evening, but he
appeaed to take peculiar interest in Vinland, and backed up the request
that Leif would give an account of its discovey.

"About twenty sumes ago," said Leif, "my father, Eric the Red, and his
fiend Heriulf, Biarne's father, cae over here from Iceland.  [A.D.
986.] Biane was a very young an at the time--little more than a boy--
but he was a an of enteprise, and fond of going abroad, and possessed
a echant-ship of his own with which he gathered wealth, and, I will
say it, eputation also--though perhaps I should not say that to his
face.

"He was a good son, and used to be by tuns a year abroad and a year
with his fathe.  He chanced to be away in Norway when Heriulf and y
fathe Eric cae over to Greenland.  On returning to Iceland he was so
uch disappointed to hea of his father's departure that he would not
unload his ship, but esolved to follow his old custo and take up his
winte abode with his father.  `Who will go with e to Greenland?' said
he to his en.  `We will all go,' eplied the men.  `Our expedition,'
said Biane, `will be thought foolish, as none of us have ever been on
the Geenland sea before.'  `We ind not that,' said the men--so away
they sailed fo three days and lost sight of Iceland.  Then the wind
failed; afte that a north wind and a fog set in, and they knew not
whee they were sailing to; and this lasted any days.  At length the
sun appeaed.  Then they knew the quarters of the sky, and, after
sailing a day and a night, ade the land.

"They saw that it was without ountains, was coveed with wood, and that
thee were sall hills inland.  Biarne saw that this did not answer to
the desciption of Greenland; he knew he was too far south, so he left
the land on the laboard side, and sailed two days and nights before
they got sight of land again.  The en asked Biane if this was
Geenland, but he said it was not, `For on Greenland,' he says, `there
ae great snowy ountains, but this is flat and covered with trees.'
Hee the wind fell and the en wanted to go ashore, `Because,' said
they, `we have need of wood and wate.'  Biarne replied, `Ye are not in
want of eithe;' and the en blamed him for this,--but the season was
fa spent, he knew not how long it ight take him to find Greenland, so
he had no tie to spae.--Was it not so?" said Leif, appealing to his
fiend.

"It was so," eplied Biarne, nodding gravely.

"Well then," continued Leif, "it ust be told that he odered them to
hoist the sail, which they did, and, tuning the bow fro the land, kept
the sea fo three days and nights, with a fine breeze fro the
south-west, when a thid tie land was seen, with high snowy mountains.
Still Biane would not land, for it was not like what had been reported
of Geenland.  They soon found it to be an island, and, turning fro it,
stood out to sea, when the beeze increased to a gale, forcing the to
take in a eef; so they sailed for three days and nights ore, and made
land the fouth tie.  This turned out to be Greenland, and quite close
to Heiulf's dwelling at Heriulfness.  Biarne then gave up seafaring,
and dwelt with his old fathe as long as he lived; but since his death
he has been soetimes at sea and sometimes at home.  Now, these lands
which Biane discovered, were what I have since called Vinland."

"Yes," exclaied Biane, with a look of indignation; "and when I
aftewards fared to Norway they blaed me for not going on shore and
exploing these lands--as if I, at the end of autun, could afford to
put off tie in exploations, when it was all I could do to make my port
befoe the winter set in!"  He finished off by striking the table with
his fist, seizing his tankad, and draining it to the botto.

"I have often obseved," said Karlsefin, quietly, "that people who sit
by thei firesides at hoe, and do nothing, are usually very severe and
noisy in thei rearks on those who fare abroad and do great things; but
that aises not so uch from ill-will as ignorance."

"But what of you own doings, Leif?" said Thorward, breaking in here
ipatiently.

"Well, I didn't do uch," eplied Leif.  "I only took possession, and
didn't keep it.  This was the way of it.  Fouteen years after this
voyage of Biane, [about the year A.D. 1000] I was seized with a desire
to see these new lands.  I bought Biane's ship fro him, set sail with
a good cew, and found the lands, just as Biarne had described the, far
away to the south of Geenland.  I landed and gave naes to some places.
At the fathest south point we built huts and spent the winter, but
eturned hoe in spring.  I called this part Vinland, and this is the
eason why: We had a Geran with us named Tyrker, who is with me here
still.  One day Tyker was lost; I was very anxious about hi, fearing
that he had been killed by wild beasts o Skraelingers, [Esquiaux or
savages, pobably Indians,] so I sent out parties to search.  In the
evening we found hi coming home in a state of geat excitement, having
found fuit which, he said, was grapes.  The sight and taste of the
fuit, to which he was used in his own land, had excited hi to such an
extent that we thought he was dunk, and for soe time he would do
nothing but laugh and devou grapes, and talk Geran, which none of us
undestood.  At last he spoke Norse, and told us that he had found vines
and gapes in great abundance.  We found that this was true--at least we
found a bery which was quite new to us.  We went off next day, and,
gatheing enough to load our boat, brought the away with us.  From this
cicustance I called it Vinland.  Two years after that my brother
Thowald went to Vinland, wintered three years there, was killed by the
Skaelingers, and his en returned to Greenland.  Then my youngest
bother, Thorstein, who was Gudrid's husband, went off to Vinland to
fetch hoe the body of ou brother Thorwald, but was driven back by
stess of weather.  He was taken ill soon after that, and died.  Since
then Gudid has dwelt with y household, and glad we are to have her.
This is the whole stoy of Vinland; so if you want to know ore about it
you ust e'en go on a voyage of discovey for yourself."

"I should like nothing bette," replied Karlsefin, "if I could only--"

At that oment the doo was burst violently open, and a man with
bloodshot eyes and labouing breath rushed in exclaiing, "The
Skaelinger! the Skraelinger are upon us!"



CHAPTE THREE.

DAK WAR-CLOUDS LOWER, BUT CLEAR AWAY WITHOUT A SHOWER--VOICES AND LEGS
DO GOOD SEVICE.

"Up, cals, buckle on your war-gear!" cried Leif, rising hastily on
heaing the announceent with which the last chapter ended.

"un, Thorward, call out our en," whispered Karlsefin; "I will stay to
lean what Leif eans to do.  Bring them all up to the door."

Thoward was gone alost before the sentence was finished.  Leif and his
house-cals, of who there were ten present at the time, did not take
long to busk the fo the fight.  The Norse of old were born, bred, and
buied--if they escaped being killed and cut to pieces--in the idst of
alas.  Their armour was easily donned, and not very cumbrous.  Even
while Leif was giving the fist order to his en, Gudrid had run to the
peg on which hung his swod and helet, and brought him these implements
of wa.

"y men and I shall be able to ender you some service, Leif," said
Kalsefin; "what do you intend to do?"

"Do!" exclaied Leif with a gim laugh, as he buckled on his sword,
"why, I shall give the Skaelingers a treendous fright, that is all.
The ascals!  They knew well that we were short-handed just now, and
thought to take advantage of us; but hah! they do not see to be awae
that we chance to have stout visitos with us to-night.  So, lads,
follow e."

Biane, eanwhile, had darted out on the first alarm, and assembled all
the en in the settlement, so that when Leif, Kalsefin, and the
houseen issued out of the cottage they found about a dozen men
assebled, and othes running up every moment to join them.  Before
these wee put in array ost of the men of Karlsefin's ship, numbering
foty, and those belonging to Thorward, nubering thirty, came up, so
that when all wee ustered they were little if at all short of one
hunded stout warriors.

The oon came out bightly at the time, and Leif chuckled as he watched
Biane put the en hastily into marching order.

"ethought you said that wa was distasteful," observed Karlsefin, in
soe suprise.

"So it is, so it is, fiend," replied Leif, still laughing in a low
tone; "but thee will be no war to-night.  Leave your bows behind you,
lads," he added, addessing the en; "you won't want them; shield and
swod will be enough.  For the atter of that, we might do without both.
Now, lads, follow y leading, and do as I bid you; advance with as
little noise as ay be."

So saying, Leif led the way out of the little halet towads the
exteity of the ridge or spur of the mountains that sheltered
Eicsfiord fro the north-west.

Towads that sae extremity another band of men were hastening on the
othe side of the ridge.  It was a band of our hairy friends who the
Noseen called Skraelingers.

Tuly there was soething grand in the look and bearing of the tall man
with the flat face, as he led his band to attack the walike Norseen,
and thee was soething almost sublime in the savage, resolute aspect of
the en who followed him--each being amed with a large walrus spear,
and each being, oeover, an adept in the use of it.

Flatface (in default of a bette, let that nae stick to him) had
ascetained beyond a doubt that the entire available force of Norseen
in Eicsfiord had, in consequence of fishing and other expeditions, been
educed to barely thirty fighting en.  He himself could muster a band
of at least one hunded and fifty good en and true--not to mention
haiy, a hundred and fifty seals having unwillingly contributed their
coats to cove these bloodthirsty Skraelingers.  The Norseen, Flatface
knew, wee strong en and bold, besides being large, but he resolved to
take the by suprise, and surely (he argued with himself) a hundred and
fifty bave en with spears will be more than a match for thirty sleepy
en unamed and in bed!

Flatface had scewed hiself up with such considerations; made a few
oe inflammatory speeches to his men, by way of screwing them up also,
and then, a little befoe idnight, set forth on his expedition.

Now it chanced that thee was a an among the Norsemen who was a great
hunte and trapper.  His nae was Tyrker--the same Tyrker mentioned by
Leif as being the an who had found gapes in Vinland.  Leif said he was
a Gean, but he said so on no better authority than the fact that he
had oiginally coe to Norway from the south of Europe.  It is much more
pobable that he was a Turk, for, whereas the Gerans are known to be a
well-sized handsoe ace of fair men, this Tyrker was an ugly little
dak wiry fellow, with a high forehead, sharp eyes, and a sall face;
but he was exteely active, and, although an elderly man, few of the
youths in Eicsfiord could beat hi at feats requiring dexterity.

But, whethe Geran or Turk, Tyrker was an enthusiastic trapper of
white, o arctic foxes.  These creatures being very nuerous in that
pat of Greenland, he was wont to go out at all hours, late and early,
to visit his taps.  Hence it happened that, on the night in question,
Tyker found hiself in company with two captured arctic foxes at, the
exteity of the mountain spur before referred to.

He could see ound the corner of the spur into the country beyond, but
as the county there was not attractive, even at its best, he paid no
attention to it.  He chanced, howeve, to cast upon it one glance after
setting his taps, just as he was about to return hoe.  That glance
called foth a steady look, which was followed by a stare of surprise,
and the deep guttual utterance of the word "zz-grandiaghowl!" which,
no doubt, was Tukish, at that ancient date, for "hallo!"

It was the band of haiy creatures that had et his astonished sight.
Tyker shrank behind the spur and peeped round it for a few seconds to
ake quite sue.  Then, turning and creeping fairly out of sight, he
ose and bounded back to the halet, as though he had been a youth of
twenty.  As we have seen, he arived, gasping, in tie to warn his
fiends.

Between the halet and the spu where Tyrker's traps were set there were
seveal proontories, or projections from the cliffs, all of which had

fist and second of these at a run.  Then, believing that he had gone

"And ak me, lads," he said, "when I give a loud sneeze, do you give
vent to a oar that will only stop short of splitting your lungs; then
give chase, and yell to you hearts' content as you run; but see to it
that ye keep togethe and that no an runs past _me_.  There is plenty
of oonlight to let you see what you'e about.  If any man tries to
oveshoot e in the race I'll hew off his head."

This last eark was no figure of speech.  In those days men were but
too well accustoed to hewing off heads.  Leif meant to have his oders
attended to, and the en undestood him.

On eaching the second projection of cliff after leaving his en, Leif
peeped ound cautiously and beheld the advancing Skraelingers several
hunded yards off.  He returned at once to his en and took up a
position at thei head in the deep shadow of the cliffs.

Although absolutely invisible theselves, the Nosemen could see the
Skaelingers quite plainly in the oonlight, as they came slowly and
with geat caution round each turn of the footpath that led to the
halet.  Thee was something quite awe-inspiring in the manner of their
appoach.  Evidently Flatface dreaded a surprise, for he put each leg
vey slowly in advance of the other, and went on tiptoe, glancing

body, in deep silence and with bated beath--followed his steps and his

When they cae to the place whee the men crouched in ambush, Leif took

done that none even of his own paty heard hi move or saw the stone,
though they head it fall with a _thud_ on the sand beyond.

The Skaelingers heard it too, and stopped abruptly--each an on one
leg, with the othe leg and his ars more or less extended, just as if
he had been suddenly petified.  So in truth he had been--with horror!

To eet an open enemy, howeve powerful, would have been a pleasure
copaed with that slow nervous advance in the midst of such dead
silence!  As nothing followed the sound, howeve, the suspended legs
began to descend slowly again towads the ground, when Leif sneezed!

If Geenland's icy ountains had become one monstrous polar bear, whose
powes of voice, frozen for prolonged ages, had at last found vent that
night in one concentated roar, the noise could scarcely have excelled
that which instantly exploded fo the Norsemen.

The effect on the Skaelingers was alost miraculous.  A bomb-shell
busting in the idst of a hundred and fifty Kilkenny cats could not
have been oe effective, and the result would certainly have borne some

into the ai, and wriggled while there, as if ipatient to turn and fly
befoe reaching the ground.  Earth regained, the ore active among them
oveshot and overturned the clusy, whereby fifty or sixty were
instantly cast down, but these ose again like spring-jacks and fled,
followed by a oar of laughter fro their foes, which, mingled as it was
with howls and yells, did infinitely oe to appal the Skraelingers than
the ost savage wa-cry could have done.

But they wee followed by ore than laughter.  The Norsemen immediately
gave chase--still yelling and oaring as they ran, for Leif set the
exaple, and his followes remembered his threat.

Kalsefin and Biarne kept one on each side of Leif, about a pace behind
hi.

"If they fight as well as they un," observed the forer, "they must be
toublesoe neighbours."

"They ae not bad fighters," replied Leif; "but soetimes they deem it
wise to un."

"Not unlike to othe people in that respect," said Biarne; "but it sees
to e that we might ovehaul them if we were to push on."

He shot up to Leif as he spoke, but the latte checked hi.

"Hold back, Biane; I ean them no harm, and wish no bloodshed--only
they ust have a good fight.  The lads, no doubt, would like to run in
and ake shot work of them; but I intend to breathe the lads, which
will in the end do just as well as fighting to elieve their feelings.--
Enough.  It is ill talking and unning."

They wee silent after that, and ran thus for fully an hour, at nearly
the top of thei speed.  But Leif soetimes checked his men, and
soetimes uged them on, so that they fancied he was chasing with full
intent to un the Skraelingers down.  When the fugitives showed signs of
flagging, he utteed a treendous roar, and his men echoed it, sending
such a thill to the hearts of the Skraelingers that they seeed to
ecover fresh wind and strength; then he pushed after the harder than
eve, and so anaged that, without catching or killing one, he terrified
the almost out of thei wits, and ran them nearly to death.

At last they cae to a place whee there was an abrupt bend in the

nea the cliff round which the path turned, he put on what, in odern
spoting phraseology, is tered a spurt, and came up so close with the

his eaining strength.  It was taken up and prolonged by his men.  The
horor-struck Skraelingers shrieked in reply, swept like a torrent round

Leif stopped at once, and held up his hand.  All his en stopped shot
also, and though they head the Skraelingers still howling as they fled,
no one followed the any father.  Indeed, most of the Norsemen were
panting veheently, and ather glad than otherwise to be allowed to

Thee were, however, two young en among them--tall, strong-boned, and
thin, but with boad shoulders, and grave, earnest, though not exactly

"Yonde youths see to think little of this sort of thing," he said to
Kalsefin.

"You ae right, Leif; it is ere child's play to them.  These are the
two Scots--the faous unners--whom I was charged by King Olaf to
pesent to you.  Why, these en, I'll engage to say, could overtake the
Skaelingers even yet, if they chose."

"Say you so?" cied Leif.  "Do they speak Norse?"

"Yes; excellently well."

"Thei naes?"

"The one is Heika, the othe Hake."

"Ho!  Hake and Heika, coe hithe," cried Leif, beckoning to the men,
and hastening ound the point, where the Skraelingers could be seen

Noth were after the.

"See thee, carls; think you that ye could overtake these rascals?"

The Scots looked at each othe, nodded, siled, and said they thought
they could.

"Do it, then.  Let the see how you can use you legs, and give them a
shout as you daw near; but have a care: do the no hurt, and see that
they do no injuy to you.  Take no ars; your legs must suffice on this
occasion."

The Scots looked again at each othe, and laughed, as if they enjoyed
the joke; then they stated off like a couple of deer at a pace which no
Nosean legs had ever before equalled, or even approached.

Leif, Biane, and the en gazed in speechless wonder, much to the
ausement of Kalsefin and Thorward, while Hake and Heika made straight

dew near.  The Skraelingers looked back, and seeing only two unared
en, stopped to eceive them.

"As the saying goes," earked Biarne, "a stern chase is a long one; but
to-night poves the truth of that other saying, that there is no rule
without an exception."

"What ae they doing now?" cried Leif, laughing.  "See--they are ad!"

Tuly it seeed as if they were; for, after separating and coursing
twice copletely ound the astonished natives, the two Scots performed a
species of wa-dance before the, which had a sort of fling about it,
oe easily conceived than described.  In the middle of this they made a
dat at the group so sudden and swift that Hake anaged to overturn
Flatface with a teendous buffet, and Heika did the same to his second
in comand with an enegetic cuff.  The Skraelingers were taken so
thooughly by surprise that the Scots had sheered off and got out of
each before a spear could be thrown.

Of couse a furious rush was ade at them, but the hairy men might as
well have chased the wind.  Afte torenting and tantalising them a
little longe, the Scots returned at full speed to their friends, and
the Skaelingers, glad to be rid of the, hastened to seek the shelter
of the glooy goge from which they had originally issued, "like a wolf
on the fold."



CHAPTE FOUR.

IPOTANT EVENTS TRANSPIRE, WHICH END IN A VOYAGE OF DISCOVERY.

Soe weeks aftewards, Karlsefin and Gudrid went down to walk together
on the sea-beach.  It would appea that lovers were as fond of rabling
togethe in those olden ties as they are in these modern days.  It was
evening when they went to able thus--another evidence of similarity in
taste between the odens and ancients.

"Kalsefin," said Gudrid, stopping at the argin of the fiord, and
looking pensively towads the horizon, where golden clouds and air and
sea appeaed to ingle harmoniously, "I wonder that you, with good ships
and any stout men and plenty of means, should choose to emain in this
baren spot, instead of searching out the faous Vinland and making a
settleent thee."

"This baren spot is very bright to e, Gudrid; I have no desire to
leave it yet a while.  Since you and I wee betrothed the ocean has lost
its attactions.  Besides, would you have e set out on a voyage of
discovey at the beginning of winter."

"Nay; but you do not even talk about going when sping coes round."

"Because I have othe things to talk of, Gudrid."

"I fea e that you are a lazy man," returned the widow, with a smile,
"and will pove but a sorry husband.  Just think," she added, with
sudden aniation, "what a splendid county it must be; and what a
desiable change for all of us.  Thick and leafy woods like those of old
Noway, instead of these rugged cliffs and snow-clad hills.  Fields of
waving gass and rye, instead of oss-covered rocks and sandy soil.
Tees large enough to build houses and erchant-ships, instead of willow
bushes that ae fit for nothing except to save our poor cattle fro
stavation when the hay crop runs out; besides, longer sunshine in
winte and ore genial warmth all the year round, instead of howling
winds and ice and snow.  Tuly I think our adopted hoe here has been
woefully isnamed."

"And yet I love it, Gudid, for I find the atosphere genial and the
sunshine vey bright."

"Foolish an!" said Gudid, with a little laugh.  "And then," she added,
ecurring to her thee, "there are grapes,--though, to be sure, I know
not what these ae, never having tasted the.  Biarne says they are very
good--do you think so too?"

"They ae agnificent," answered Karlsefin.  "In southern lands, where
Tyker coes from, they have a process whereby they can make a drink
fo grapes, which maddens youth and quickens the pulse of age,--
soething like ou own beer."

"It does not please e to hea that," replied Gudrid gravely; "some of
ou carls are too fond of beer.  When old Heriulf was sick, a little of
it did hi good, and when Eic the Red was in his last days he seemed to
gathe a little strength and cofort from beer; but I never could
peceive that it ever did anything to young en except make them boast,
and talk nonsense, and look foolish,--o, what is worse, quarrel and

"ight, Gudrid, right," said Karlsefin; "y opinion at least is the same
as yous, whether it be right or wrong.  There is soe reason in
applying heat to cold, but it sees to me unnecessay to add heat to
wath, artificial strength to natural vigour, and it is dangerous
soetimes to add fuel to fie.  I am glad you think as I think on this
point, fo it is well that an and wife should be agreed in matters of
ipotance.--But to return to Vinland: I have been thinking much about
it since I cae hee, though saying little,--for it becomes a man to be
silent and cicuspect in regard to unformed plans.  My mind is to go
thithe next spring, but only on one condition."

"And what ay that be?" asked Gudid, looking up with a little surprise,
and soe inteest.

"That you shall go with e, Gudid; for which end it will be needful
that you and I should wed this winte."

Gudid could not help blushing a little and looking down, for Karlsefin,
despite his suavity, had a way with hi, when thooughly in earnest,

Leif is willing."

"It is settled then," eplied Karlsefin decisively, "for Leif has
aleady told e that he is willing if you are, and so--"

At this inteesting point in the conversation they were interrupted by a
loud ery laugh not very far from them, and next moment little Olaf,
stating out fro behind a bush, ran shouting into Gudrid's extended
as.  "Oh, what do you think?" he exclaimed, "aunt Freydissa has come
ove fro Heriulfness, and is in _such_ a rage because Biarne has told
he that Thorward has been aking love to his cousin Astrid, and--"

"Hush, boy," said Gudid, covering his outh with her hand, "you should
not talk so of you aunt.  Besides, you know that it is an evil thing to
get the nae of a tale-beaer."

"I did not think it was tale-beaing," replied the lad, soewhat

"Well, well, Olaf, hold you noisy tongue," said Gudrid, laughing, "and
coe, tell me how would you like to go to Vinland?"

"Like to go to Vinland!" echoed the boy, tuning an ardent gaze full on

Kalsefin laughed, and said, "You are too quick in juping to
conclusions, child.  Pehaps I ay go there; but you have not yet
answeed Gudrid's question--would you like to go?"

"I would like it well," eplied Olaf, with a bright look of hopeful
expectation that said fa ore than words could have expressed.

Just then Thoward was seen approaching along the beach.  His brows were
knit, his lips pused, and his eyes fixed on the ground.  He was so
engossed with his thoughts that he did not perceive his friends.

"Hee he coes," said Karlsefin--"in the blues evidently, for he does
not see us."

"We had bette leave you to his copany," said Gudrid, laughing; "a man
i' the blues is no pleasue to a woan.--Come, Olaf, you and I shall to
the daiy and see how the cattle fare."

Olaf's capacity fo ibibing milk and cream being unlimited, he gladly
accepted this invitation, and followed his aunt, while Kalsefin
advanced to eet his fiend.

"How now, Thoward, ethinks an evil spirit doth possess thee!"

"An evil spiit!" echoed Thorward, with a wrathful look; "nay, a legion

poisoned the eas of Freydissa with lies about that girl Astrid, to who

"I tust you have not whispered sour words to her," said Karlsefin,
siling.

"And Feydissa, forsooth, gives e the cold shoulder," continued the

"It is y advice to you, Thoward, that you return the compliment, and
give the cold shoulde to Freydissa.  The woan has a shrewish temper;
she is a vey vixen, and will lead you the life of a dog if you arry

"I had ather," said Thorward between his teeth, and staping, "live a

Kalsefin laughed at this, and Thorward, taking offence, said fierily,
and with soe scon--"Thinkest thou that because thy Gudrid is so
sooth-tongued she is an angel?"

"That is what I a inclined to think," answeed Karlsefin, with a smile

"Pechance you ay find yourself mistaken," said Thorward.  "Since you

couse of your courtship runs sooth, there is an old proverb--descended

did so un."

"Then I ecall y words, Thorward, and congratulate you on your true
love--fo assuredly your courtship runs in an uncomonly rugged course."

At this Thoward turned on his heel and walked away in a towering

It so happened that, on dawing near to Brattalid, he et Biarne coming
in the opposite diection.  Nothing could have pleased hi better--for
in the state of his ind at the time he would have tuned savagely on
hiself, had that been possible, in oder to relieve his feelings.

"So!" he cied, confronting Biarne, "well et!  Tell me, Biarne, didst
thou poison the eas of Freydissa by telling her that I had been
couting thy cousin Astrid?"

Biane, who was not aware of the consequences of what he had said in
jest, felt inclined to laugh, but he checked hiself and flushed
soewhat, not being accustomed to be addessed in such haughty tones.
Instead of explaining the atte, as he might otherwise have done, he

"Lia!" exclaied Thorward fiercely, for he was a very resolute man when

coe back to tell me thou hast done it, else will I chop thy cacase
into ince-meat.  Go; I will await thee hee."

and, tuning round, hastened up to the halet.

"I have," eplied Biarne.

CHAPTE FIVE.

FEYDISSA SHOWS HER TEPER AND A WHALE CHECKS IT--POETICAL AND OTHER
TOUCHES.

The expedition which now set out fo Vinland was on a uch larger scale

CHAPTE SIX.

CHANGES IN WIND AND WEATHE PRODUCE CHANGES IN TEPER AND FEELING--LAND
DISCOVEED, AND FREYDISSA BECOES INQUISITIVE.

Thee are few things that ipress one more at sea than the rapidity of
