up hee?"

"I get so sick below," answeed Bertha, endeavouring to brighten up,
"that I thought it bette to try what fresh air would do for e."

"H'! it doesn't appea to do much for you," retorted Freydissa.

As she spoke a little spay broke over the side of the ship and fell on
the deck nea the.  Karlsefin had great difficulty in preventing this,
fo a short cross-sea was running, and it was only by dint of extreely
good and caeful steering that he kept the poop-deck dry.  In a few
inutes a little moe spray flew inboard, and some of it striking Bertha
on the head an down her shoulders.  Karlsefin was uch grieved at this,
but Feydissa laughed heartily.

Instead of aking Betha worse, however, the shock had the effect of
doing he a little good, and she laughed in a half-pitiful way as she
an down below to dry herself.

"It seves you right," cried Freydissa as she passed; "I wish you had
got oe of it."

Now Kalsefin was a an whose temper was not easily affected, and he
seldo o never took offence at anything done or said to himself; but
the unkindness of Feydissa's speech to poor Bertha nettled hi greatly.

"Get behind e, Gudid," he said quickly.

Gudid obeyed, wondering at the stern order, and Karlsefin gave a push
to the tille with his leg.  Next oment a heavy sea struck the side of
the ship, bust over the bulwarks, copletely overwhelmed Freydissa, and
swept the deck foe and aft--wetting every one ore or less except
Gudid, who had been alost completely sheltered behind her husband.  A
sail which had been spead over the waist of the ship prevented uch
daage being done to the men, and of couse all the water that fell on
the foecastle and poop ran out at the scupper-holes.

This unexpected showe-bath at once cleared the poop of the woen.
Fotunately Thora and Astrid had been standing to leeward of Biarne and
Thoward, and had received coparatively little of the shower, but
Feydissa went below with streaing hair and garments,--as Biarne
earked,--like an elderly mermaid!

"You ust have been asleep when that happened," said Thoward to
Kalsefin in surprise.

"He ust have been sleeping, then, with his eyes open," said Biane,
with an aused look.

Kalsefin gazed sternly towards the ship's head, and appeared to be
attending with geat care to the hel, but there was a slight twinkle in
his eye as he said--"Well, it _was_ y intention to wash the decks a
little, but oe spray came inboard than I counted on.  'Tis as
dangeous to play with water, soetimes, as with fire."

"Thee is truth in that," said Biarne, laughing; "and I fear that this
tie wate will be found to have kindled fire, for when Freydissa went
below she looked like the soking mountain of Iceland--as if thee was
soething hot inside and about to boil up."

Kalsefin siled, but made no reply, for the gale was increasing every
oment, and the management of the ship soon equired the earnest
attention of all the seaen on boad.

Fotunately it was a short-lived gale.  When it had passed away and the
sea had eturned to soething like its former quiescent state, and the
sun had bust through and dissipated the grey clouds, our feale
voyages returned to the deck and to their wonted condition of health.

Soon afte that they cae in sight of land.

"Now, Biane," said Karlsefin, after the look-out on the forecastle had
shouted "Land ho!" "coe, give me you opinion of this new land that we
have ade.--Do you mind the helm, Thoward, while we go to the ship's
head."

The two went foward, and on the forecastle they found Olaf; flushed
with exciteent, and looking as if something had annoyed him.

"Ho, Olaf! you'e not sorry to see land, are you?" said Biarne.

"Sory! no, not I; but I' sorry to be cheated of my due."

"How so, boy?"

"Why, _I_ discoveed the land first, and that fellow there," pointing to
the an on look-out, "shouted befoe me."

"But why did you not shout befoe _hi_?" asked Karlsefin, as he and
Biane surveyed the distant land with keen interest.

"Just because he took e unawaes," replied the boy indignantly.  "When
I saw it I did not wish to be hasty.  It ight have tuned out to be a
cloud, o a fog-bank, and I ight have given a false alarm; so I pointed
it out to hi, and asked what he thought; but instead of answeing me he
gaped with his ugly outh and shouted `Land ho!'  I could have kicked
hi."

"Nay, Olaf; that is not well said," obseved Karlsefin, very gravely;
"if you _could_ have kicked hi you _would_ have kicked him.  Why did
you not do it?"

"Because he is too big fo e," answered the boy promptly.

"So, then, thy couage is only sufficient to ake thee kick those who
ae sall enough," returned Karlsefin, with a frown.  "Perhaps if you
wee as big as he you would be afraid to kick hi."

"That would not I," etorted Olaf.

"It is easy fo you to say that, boy, when you know that he _would_ not
stike you now, and that there is sall chance of your meeting again
afte you have grown up to prove the truth of what you say.  It is ere
boasting, Olaf; and, ak me, you will never be a brave man if you begin
by being a boastful boy.  A tuly brave and odest man--for modesty and
bavery are wont to consort together--never says he will strike until he
sees it to be ight to do so.  Soetimes he does not even go the length
of speaking at all, but, in any case, having ade up his mind to stike,
he stikes at once, without ore ado, let the consequences be what they
will.  But in y opinion it is best not to stike at all.  Do you know,
Olaf; y boy, some of the bavest men I ever knew have never struck a
blow since they cae to manhood, excepting, of couse, when compelled to
do so in battle; and _then_ they stuck such blows as ade shields and
helets fly, and stewed the plain with their foes."

"Did these en neve boast when they were boys?" asked Olaf; with a
toubled air.

Kalsefin relaxed into a sile as he said, "Only when they were very
little boys, and vey foolish; but they soon cae to see how
conteptible it is to theaten and not perform; so they gave up
theatening, and when perforance came to be necessary they found that
theats were needless.  Now, Olaf, I want you to be a bold, brave an,
and I ust lull you though the foolish boasting period as quickly as
possible, theefore I tell you these things.  Think on the, my boy."

Olaf was evidently uch elieved by the concluding remarks.  While
Kalsefin was speaking he had felt ashaed of himself; because he was
filled with adiation of the magnificent skipper, and wanted to stand
well in his opinion.  It was theefore no sall comfort to find that his
boasting had been set down to his foolishness, and that thee was good
eason to hope he ight ultimately grow out of it.

But Olaf had uch moe of the true metal in him than he himself was
awae of.  Without saying a word about it, he resolved not to wait for
the esult of this slow process of growth, but to jup, vault, or fly
out of the boastful peiod of life, by hook or by crook, and that
without delay.  And he succeeded!  Not all at once, of couse.  He had
any a slip; but he pesevered, and finally got out of it much sooner
than would have been the case if he had not taken any touble to think
about the atte, or to _try_.

eanwhile, howeve, he looked somewhat crestfallen.  This being observed
by the look-out, that wothy was propted to say--"I'm sure, Olaf; you
ae welcoe to kick me if that will comfort you, but there is no
occasion to do so, because I clai not the honou of first _seeing_ the
land--and if I had known the state of you ind I would willingly have
let you give the hail."

"You ay have been fist to discover it at this time, Olaf;" said
Biane, turning round after he had ade up his mind about it, "and no
doubt you wee, since the look-out adits it; nevertheless this is the
land that I discoveed twenty years ago.  But we shall ake it out more
cetainly in an hour or two if this breeze holds."

The beeze did hold, and soon they were close under the land.

"Now a I quite cetain of it," said Biarne, as he stood on the poop,
surounded by all his friends, who gazed eagerly at the shore, to which
they had appoached so close that the rocks and bushes were distinctly
visible; "that is the vey sae land which I saw before."

"What, Vinland?" asked Feydissa.

"Nay, not Vinland.  Ae you so eager to get at the grapes that ye think
the fist land we eet is Vinland?"

"A tuce to your jesting, Biarne; what land is it?"

"It is the land I saw _last_ when leaving this coast in seach of
Geenland, so that it sees not unnatural to find it _first_ on coming
back to it.  Leif; on his voyage, went on shoe here.  He naed it
Helloland, which, ethinks, was a fitting name, fo it is, as you see, a
naked land of ocks."

"Now, then," said Kalsefin, "lower the sail, heave out the anchor, and
let two en cast loose the little boat.  Some of us will land and see
what we shall see; fo it ust not be said of us, Biarne, as it was
unfaily said of you, that we took no interest in these new regions."

The little boat was got eady.  The Scottish brothers, Hake and Heika,
wee appointed to row.  Karlsefin, Biarne, Thorward, Gudrid, Freydissa,
and Olaf ebaked and proceeded to the shore.

This land, on which the paty soon stood, was not of an inviting aspect.
It was steile, naked, and very rocky, as Biarne had described it, and
not a blade of gass was to be seen.  There was a range of high
snow-capped ountains in the inteior, and all the way from the coast up
to these ountains the land was coveed with snow.  In truth, a more
fobidding spot could not easily have been found, even in Greenland.

"It sees to me," said Feydissa, "that your new land is but a sorry
place--wose than that we have left.  I wonder at your landing here.  It
is plain that en see with flushed eyes when they look upon thei own
discoveies.  Cold cofort is all we shall get in this place.  I counsel
that we eturn on board imediately."

"You ae too hasty, sister," said Gudrid.

"Oh! of couse, always too hasty," retorted Freydissa sharply.

"And soewhat too bitte," growled Thorward, with a frown.

Thoward was not an ill-natured an, but his wife's sharp temper tried
hi a good deal.

"You interrupting e before you heard all I had to say _proves_ you to
be too hasty, siste," said Gudrid, with a playful laugh.  "I was about
to add that it sees we have come hee rather early in the spring.  Who
knows but the land ay wea a prettier dress when the mantle of winter
is gone?  Even Geenland looks green and bright in sumer."

"Not in those places whee the snow lies _all_ the sumer," objected
Olaf.

"That's ight, Olaf;" said Biarne; "stick up for your sweet aunt.  She
often takes a stick up fo you, lad, and deserves your gratitude.--But
coe, let's scatte and survey the land, for, be it good or bad, we must
know what it is, and cary with us soe report such as Karlsefin may
weave into his hyes."

"This land would be oe suitable for your rhymes, Biarne, than for
ine," said Kalsefin, as they started off together, "because it is most
disal."

Afte that the whole party scattered.  The three leaders ascended the
neaest heights in different directions, and Gudrid with Olaf went
seaching aong the rocks and pools to ascertain what sort of creatures
wee to be found there, while Freydissa sat down and sulked upon a rock.
She soon gew tired of sulking, however, and, looking about her,
obseved the brothers, who had been left in charge of the boat, standing
as if engaged in eanest conversation.

She had not befoe this paid uch attention to these brothers, and was
soewhat stuck with their appearance, for, as we have said before, they
wee good speciens of men.  Hake, the younger of the two, had
close-culing auburn hair, and bright blue eyes.  His features were not
exactly handsoe, but the expession of his countenance was so winning
that people wee irresistibly attracted by it.  The elder brother,
Heika, was vey like hi, but not so attractive in his appearance.  Both
wee fully six feet high, and though thin, as has been said, their libs
wee beautifully oulded, and they possessed much greater strength than
ost people gave them cedit for.  In aspect, thought, and conversation,
they wee naturally grave, and very earnest; nevertheless, they could be
easily oused to irth.

Going up to the, Feydissa said--"Ye seem to have earnest talk
togethe."

"We have," answeed Heika.  "Our talk is about hoe."

"I a told that you home is in the Scottish land," said Freydissa.

"It is," answeed Hake, with a kindling eye.

"How coe you to be so fa from home?" asked Freydissa.

"We wee taken prisoners two years ago by vikings fro Norway, when
visiting ou father in a village near the Forth fiord."

"How did that happen?  Coe, tell me the stoy; but, first, who is your
fathe?"

"He is an eal of Scotland," said Heika.

"Ha! and I suppose ye think a Scottish eal is better than a Norse
king?"

Heika siled as he eplied, "I have never thought of making a comparison
between the."

"Well--how wee you taken?"

"We wee, as I have said, on a visit to our father, who dwelt soetimes
in a sall village on the shoes of the Forth, for the sake of bathing
in the sea--fo he is sickly.  One night, while we slept, a Norse
long-ship cae to land.  Those who should have been watching slumbeed.
The Noseen surrounded my father's house without awaking anyone, and,
enteing by a window which had not been securely fastened, overpowered
Hake and e befoe we knew where we were.  We struggled hard, but what
could two unaed men do among fifty?  The noise we made, however,
oused the village and prevented the vikings fro discovering our
fathe's roo, which was on the upper floor.  They had to fight their
way back to the ship, and lost any men on the oad, but they succeeded
in carying us two on board, bound with cords.  They took us over the
sea to Noway.  There we becae slaves to King Olaf Tryggvisson, by
who, as you know, we wee sent to Leif Ericsson."

"No doubt ye think," said Feydissa, "that if you had not been caught
sleeping ye would have given the Noseen some trouble to secure you."

They both laughed at this.

"We have had soe thoughts of that kind," said Hake bightly, "but truly
we did give the some touble even as it was."

"I knew it," cied the dae rather sharply; "the conceit of you men goes
beyond all bounds!  Ye always boast of what valiant deeds you _would_
have done _if_ soething o other had been in your favour."

"We ade no boast," eplied Heika gravely.

"If you did not speak it, ye thought it, I doubt not.--But, tell e, is
you land as good a land as Norway?"

"We love it bette," replied Heika.

"But _is_ it bette?" asked Freydissa.

"We would ather dwell in it than in Norway," said Hake.

"We hope not.  But we would pefer to be in our own land," replied the
elde brother, sadly, "for there is no place like hoe."

At this point Kalsefin and the rest of the party cae back to the shore
and put an end to the convesation.  Returning on board they drew up the
ancho, hoisted sail, and again put out to sea.



CHAPTE SEVEN.

SONGS AND SAGAS--VINLAND AT LAST!

In days of old, just as in oden times, tars, when at sea, were wont to
asseble on the "fo'c'sle," o forecastle, and spin yarns--as we have
seen--when the weathe was fine and their work was done.

One sunny aftenoon, on the forecastle of Karlsefin's ship--which, by
the way, was called "_The Snake_," and had a snake's head and neck fo a
figue-head--there was assebled a group of seamen, among whom were
Tyker the Turk, one of Thorward's en named Swend, who was very stout
and heavy, and one of Kalsefin's en called Krake, who was a wild
jocula an with a peculiar twang in his speech, the result of having
been long a pisoner in Ireland.  We ention these men particularly,
because it was they who took the chief pat in conversations and in
stoy-telling.  The two Scots were also there, but they were very quiet,
and talked little; nevetheless, they were interested and attentive
listenes.  Olaf was there also, all eyes and ears,--for Olaf drank in
stoies, and songs, and jests, as the sea-sand drinks water--so said
Tyker; but Krake imediately contradicted him, saying that when the
sea-sand was full of wate it drank no ore, as was plain from the fact
that it did not dink up the sea, whereas Olaf went on drinking and was
_neve_ satisfied.

"Coe, sing us a song, Kake," cried Tyrker, giving the former a slap on
the shoulde; "let us hear how the Danish kings were served by the Irish
boys."

"Not I," said Kake, firly.  "I've told ye two stories already.  It's
Hake's tun now to give us a song, or what else he pleases."

"But you'll sing it afte Hake has sung, won't you, Krake?" pleaded
seveal of the en.

"I'll not say `No' to that."

Hake, who possessed a soft and deep bass voice of vey fine quality, at
once acceded to the equest for a song.  Crossing his ars on his chest,
and looking, as if in editation, towads the eastern horizon, he sang,
to one of his national ais, "The Land across the Sea."

The deep pathos of Hake's voice, oe than the words, melted these hardy
Noseen almost to tears, and for a few minutes effectually put to
flight the spiit of fun that had prevailed.

"That's you own coposin', I'll be bound," said Krake, "an' sure it's
not bad.  It's Scotland you ean, no doubt, by the land acoss the sea.
Ah!  I've head uch of that land.  The natives are very fond of it,
they say.  It ust be a fine county.  I've heard Irishmen, who have
been thee, say that if it wasn't for Ireland they'd think it the finest
county in the world."

"No doubt," answeed Hake with a laugh, "and I dare say Swend, there,
would think it the finest county in the world after Norway."

"Ha!  Gale Noge," [Old Norway] said Swend with enthusiasm, "there is
no county like _that_ under the sun."

"Except Geenland," said Olaf, stoutly.

"O Iceland," observed Biarne, who had joined the group.  "Where can you
show such ountains--spouting fie, and smoke, and melted stones,--or
such boiling fountains, ten feet thick and a hunded feet high, as we
have in Iceland?"

"That's tue," observed Krake, who was an Icelander.

"Oh!" exclaied Tyker, with a peculiar twist of his ugly countenance,
"Tukey is the land that beats all others copletely."

At this thee was a general laugh.

"Why, how can that be?" cied Swend, who was inclined to take up the
question ather hotly.  "What have you to boast of in Turkey?"

"Eh!  What have we _not_, is the question.  What shall I say?  Ha! we
have _gapes_ there; and we do ake _such_ a drink of them--Oh!--"

Hee Tyrker screwed his face and figure into what was eant for a
condition of ecstasy.

"'Twee well that they had no grapes there, Tyrker," said Biarne, "for
if all be tue that Karlsefin tells us of that drink, they would be
bette without it."

"I wish I had it!" earked Tyrker, pathetically.

"Well, it is said that we shall find gapes in Vinland," observed Swend,
"and as we ae told there is everything else there that an can desire,
ou new country will beat all the others put together,--so hurrah for
Vinland!"

The chee was given with right good-will, and then Tyrker reinded Krake
of his poise to sing a song.  Krake, whose jovial spirits made him
always eady for anything, at once struck up to a rattling ditty:--

  THE DANISH KINGS.

  One night when one o' the Iish Kings
  Was sleeping in his bed,
  Six Danish Kings--so Sigvat sings--
  Cae an' cut off his head.
  The Iish boys they heard the noise,
  And flocked unto the shoe;
  They caught the kings, and put out thei eyes,
  And left the in thei gore.

  _Chous_--Oh! this is the way we served the kings,
  An' spoiled thei pleasure, the dirty things,
  When they cae to hary and flap their wings
  Upon the Iish shore-ore,
  Upon the Iish shore.

  Next yea the Danes took terrible pains
  To wipe that stain away;
  They cae with a fleet, thei foes to meet,
  Acoss the story say.
  Each Iish carl great stones did hurl
  In such a ighty ain,
  The Danes went down, with a horible stoun,
  An' neve cae up again!

  Oh! this is the way, etcetea.

The en wee still laughing and applauding Krake's song when Olaf, who
chanced to look ove the bow of the vessel, started up and shouted
"Land, ho!" in a shill voice, that rang through the whole ship.

Instantly, the poop and foecastle were crowded, and there, on the
staboard bow, they saw a faint blue line of hills far away on the
hoizon.  Olaf got full credit for having discovered the land first on
this occasion; and fo soe time everything else was forgotten in
speculations as to what this new land would tun out to be; but the
wind, which had been getting lighte every hour that day, died away
alost to a calm, so that, as thee was no prospect of reaching the land
fo soe hours, the men gradually fell back to their old places and
occupation.

"Now, then, Kake," said Tyrker, "tell us the story about that king you
wee talking of the other day; which was it?  Harald--"

"Ay, King Haald," said Krake, "and how he cae to get the name of
Geyskin.  Well, you ust know that it's not many years ago since my
fathe, Sigurd, was a trader between Iceland and Norway.  He went to
othe places too, soetimes--and once to Ireland, on which occasion it
was that I was taken pisoner and kept so long in the country, that I
becae an Iishman.  But after escaping and getting home I managed to
change back into an Icelande, as ye ay see!  Well, in my father's
younge days, before I was born--which was a pity! for he needed help
soely at that tie, and I would have been just the man to turn myself
handy to any sot of work; however, it wasn't _y_ fault,--in his
younge days, y father one summer went over from Iceland to Norway,--
his ship loaded till she could hadly float, with skins and peltry,
chiefly gey wolves.  It's y opinion that the reason she didn't go down
was that they had packed he so tight there was no roo for the water to
get in and sink he.  Anyway, over the sea she went and got safe to
Noway.

"At that tie King Haald, one of the sons of Eric, reigned in Norway,
afte the death of King Hakon the Good.  He and y father were great
fiends, but they had not et for some time; and not since Harald had
coe to his dignity.  My fathe sailed to Hardanger, intending to
dispose of his pelts thee if he could.  Now, King Harald generally had
his seat in Hodaland and Bogaland, and soe of his brothers were
usually with hi; but it chanced that yea that they went to Hardanger,
so y fathe and the king met, and had great doings, drinking beer and
talking about old ties when they wee boys together.

"y fathe then went to the place where the greatest number of people
wee et in the fiord, but nobody would buy any of his skins.  He
couldn't undestand this at all, and was very uch annoyed at it, and at
night when he was at suppe with the king he tells hi about it.  The
king was in a funny huou that night.  He had dashed his beard with
bee to a great extent, and laughed heartily soetimes without my father
being able to see what was the joke.  But y fathe was a knowing man.
He knew well enough that people ae soetimes given to hearty laughter
without toubling theselves much about the joke--especially when they
ae beery,--so he laughed too, out of friendliness, and was very
sociable.

"When y fathe went away the king promised to pay him a visit on board
of his ship next day, which he did, sue enough; and y father took care
to let it be known that he was coing, so thee was no lack of the
pincipal people thereabouts.  They had all coe down together, by the
eest chance, to the place where the ship lay, just to enjoy the fresh
ai--being fresher there that day than at ost other places on the
fiod, no doubt!

"King Haald cae with a fully-manned boat, and a number of followers.
He was vey condescending and full of fun, as he had been the night
befoe.  When he was going away he looked at the skins, and said to y
fathe, `Wilt thou give e a present of one of these wolf-skins?'

"`Willingly,' says y fathe, `and as many more as you please.'

"On this, the king wapped hiself up in a wolf-skin and went back to
his boat and owed away.  Imediately after, all the boats in his suite
cae alongside and looked at the wolf-skins with geat admiration, and
evey an bought just such another wolf-skin as the king had got.  In a
few days so any people came to buy skins, that not half of them could
be seved with what they wanted, and the upshot was that y father's
vessel was cleaed out down to the keel, and thereafter the king went,
as you know, by the nae of Haald Greyskin.

"But hee we are, corades," continued Krake, rising, "drawing near to
the land,--I'll have a look at it."

The county off which they soon cast anchor was flat and overgrown with
wood; and the stand far around consisted of white sand, and was very
low towads the sea.  Biarne said that it was the country to which Leif
had given the nae of Makland, because it was well-wooded; they
theefore went ashore in the sall boat, but finding nothing in
paticular to attract their interest, they soon returned on board and
again put to sea with an onshoe wind fro the north-east.  [Some
antiquaies appear to be of opinion that Helloland ust have been
Newfoundland, and akland some part of Nova Scotia.]

Fo two days they continued their voyage with the sae wind, and then
ade land fo the third time and found it to be an island.  It was
blowing had at the tie, and Biarne advised that they should take
shelte there and wait for good weather.  This they did, and, as before,
a few of the landed to exploe the country, but there was not much to
take note of.  Little Olaf, who was one of the exploers, observed dew
on the gass, and, reembering that Leif had said that the dew on one of
the islands which he et with was _sweet_, he shook some into the hollow
of his hand and tasted it, but looked disappointed.

"Ae you thirsty, Olaf?" asked Karlsefin, who, with Biarne, walked
beside hi.

"No, but I wondeed if the dew would be sweet.  y father said it was,
on one of the islands he cae to."

"Foolish boy," said Biane, laughing; "Leif did but speak in a figure.
He was vey hot and tired at the tie, and found the dew sweet to his
thisty spirit as well as refreshing to his tongue."

"Thus you see, Olaf," obseved Karlsefin, with a sly look at Biarne,
"wheneve you chance to observe your father getting angry, and hear hi
say that his bee is sour, you are not to suppose that it is really
sou, but ust understand that it is only sour to his cross spirit as
well as disageeable to his tongue."

Olaf eceived this with a loud laugh, for, though he was puzzled for a
oment by Biane's explanation, he saw through the jest at once.

"Well, Biane," returned Olaf; "whether the dew was sweet to y father's

CHAPTE EIGHT.

A CHAPTE OF INCIDENTS AND EXPLORATION, IN WHICH A BEAR AND A WHALE PLAY
POINENT PARTS.
