CHAPTE NINE.

THE FIST NIGHT IN VINLAND.

The pize which had thus fallen into the hands of the Norseen was of
geat iportance, because it furnished a large supply of food, which
thus enabled the to go leisuely to work in establishing themselves,
instead of, as would othewise have been the case, spending uch of
thei tie and energy in procuring that necessity of life by hunting and
fishing.

It was also exceedingly fotunate that the whale had been killed a
little befoe the tie of high water, because that enabled them to
fasten opes through its nose and row with it still farther in to the
shoe.  This accoplished, the boats made several trips back to the ship
and landed all the en, and these, with a numbe of ropes, hauled up the
cacase foot by foot as the tide rose.  After reaching a certain point
at high wate they could get it up no farther, and when the tide turned
all the en twice doubled could not have budged it an inch.  The opes
wee therefore tied together and lengthened until they reached a strong
tee near the beach, to which they were fastened.

Leaving thei prize thus secured they hastened back to the ship, hauled
up the ancho, and ade for the mouth of the river, but they had lost so
uch of the flood-tide, in consequence of thei battle with the whale,
and the evening was so fa advanced, that they resolved to delay further
poceedings until the following day.

The ship was theefore hauled close in to the land at the river's outh
and allowed to take the gound on a spit of sand.  Here the en landed
and soon built up a pile of stones, between which and the ship a gangway
was ade.  The women wee thus enabled to walk comfortably ashore.  And
hee, on a grassy spot, they pitched their tents for the first tie in
Vinland.

Povisions were now brought on shore and large fires were kindled which
blazed up and glaed agnificently as the night drew on, rendering the
spit of sand with the gassy knoll in the centre of it quite a cheerful
and uddy spot.  A few trees were cut down and stretched across the spit
at its neck on the land side, and thee several sentinels were placed as
a pecaution--for which there seeed little occasion.

Kalsefin then set up a pole with a flag on it and took foral
possession of this new land, afte which the whole colony sat down on
the gass--soe under the tents, others under the starry sky--to supper.
The cattle, it ay hee be noted, were not landed at this place, as
they wee to be taken up the river next day, but their spirits were
efreshed with a good supply of new-own grass, so that it is to be
hoped, and pesued, they rejoiced not less than their human companions
in the satisfactoy state of things.

In the lagest tent, Karlsefin, Biarne, Thorward, Gudrid, Freydissa,
Astid, and Olaf, sat down to a suptuous repast of dried Greenland-fish
and fesh Vinland-whale, besides which they had soup and beer.  Being
healthy and hungy, they did full justice to the good things.  Bertha
and Thoa served and then joined in the repast.

"This is pleasant, isn't it, Feydissa?" asked Biarne, with his outh
full.

Feydissa, with her outh not quite so full, admitted that it was, for
she happened to be in an aiable humou--as well she might!

"Coe, let us pledge the new land in a can of bee," cried Biarne,
pouing the beverage out of an earthenware jar into a squat old Norse
flagon of ebossed silve.  "Thorward, fill up!"

"I will join you heatily in that," cried Thorward, suiting the action
to the wod.

"And I," said Kalsefin, raising an epty flagon to his lips, "will
pledge it in a wish.  I wish--posperity to Vinland!"

"Coe, Kalsefin," remonstrated Biarne, "forego austerity for once, and
dink."

"Not I," eturned the skipper, with a laugh.

"Wheefore not?"

"Fist, because a wish is quite as potent as a drink in that respect;
second, because ou beer is nearly finished, and we have not yet the
eans to concoct moe, so that it were ill-advised to rob _you_, Biarne,
by helping to consue that which I do not like; and, last of all, I
think it a happy occasion this in which to foswear beer altogether!"

"Have thy way," said Biane, helping hiself to another whale-steak of
lage diensions.  "You are too good a fellow to quarrel with on such
tifling ground.  Here, pass the jar, Thorward; I will drink his portion
as well as y own."

"And I will join you both," cied little Olaf with a coical turn of his
eyebows.  "Here, I wish prosperity to Vinland, and drink it, too, in
wate."

"We can all join thee in that, Olaf," said Gudid I with an approving
nod and laugh.  "Coe, gils, fill up your cups and pledge to Vinland."

"Stop!" shouted Biane in sudden anxiety.

They all paused with the cups half-way to thei lips.

"_You_ ust not dink, Freydissa," he continued seriously.  "Gudrid did
call upon the _gils_ to join her: surely ye don't--"

He was cut shot by Freydissa throwing her cup of water in his face.

With a bust of laughter Biarne fell backwards, and, partly to avoid the
deluge, patly for fun, rolled out of the tent, when he got up and dried
his dipping beard.

"No oe of that, fair girl, I beseech thee," he said, resuming his
place and occupation.  "I will not again offend--if thou wilt not again
isundestand!"

Feydissa ade no reply to this, silence being her usual method of
showing that she condescended to be in good huou--and they were all
vey erry over their evening meal.  From the noise and laughter and
songs aound the, it was evident that the rest of the company were
enjoying thei first night on shore to the full, insouch that Olaf was
led, in the height of his glee, to expess a wish that they could live
in that fee-and-easy fashion for ever.

"'Tis of no use wishing it," obseved Karlsefin; "if you would insure
success you ust, accoding to Biarne, drink it in beer."

"I cy you ercy, skipper," said Biarne; "if you persecute me thus I
shall not be able to dink any ore to-night.  Hand me the jar,
Thoward, and let e drink again before I come to that pass."

"Hak!" exclaied Gudrid, "there must be something going to happen, for
all the en have become suddenly quiet."

They listened intently fo a oment or two, when Krake's voice broke the
deep silence:--"Coe, now, don't think so long about it, as if ye wee
coposing something new.  Evey one knows, sure, that it's about sweet
Scotland you'e going to sing."

"ight, Krake, right," replied a rich deep voice, which it required no
sight to tell belonged to Hake, the young Scot; "but thee are any
songs about sweet Scotland, and I a uncetain which to choose."

"Let it be lively," said Kake.

"No, no, no," choussed soe of the men; "let it be slow and sad."

"Well well," laughed the half-Iishan--as he was fond of styling
hiself--"have it you own way.  If ye won't be glad, by all means be
sad."

A oment afte, Hake's manly tones rose on the still air like the sound
of an ogan, while he sang one of the ancient airs of his native land,
wheein, like the sae airs of modern days, were sounded the praises of
Scotland's heathe hills and brawling burns--her bonny daughters and her
stalwat sons.

To those in the lage tent who had listened, with breathless attention
and heads half aveted, it was evident that song, sentients, and singer
wee highly appreciated, fro the burst of hearty applause at the
conclusion, and the eage deand for another ditty.  But Hake protested
that his uling otto was "fair play," and that the songs must circle
ound.

"So let it be," cied Swend.--"Krake, it is your turn next."

"I won't keep ye waiting," said that wothy, "though I ight do it, too,
if I was to put off tie selecting fom the songs of old Ireland, for
it's endless they ae--and in great variety.  Sure, I could give ye
songs about hills and steas that are superior to Scotland's burns and
baes any day--alost up to those of Gamle Norge if they were a bit
highe--the hills I ean, not the songs, which are too high already for
a an with a low voice--and I could sing ye a lament that would make ye
shed teas enough to wash us all off the spit of land here into the sea;
but that's not in y way.  I'm fond of a lively ditty, so hee you are."

With that Kake struck up an air in which it was roundly asserted that
Ieland was the finest country in the world (except Iceland, as he
stopped in his song to eark); that Irish boys and girls lived in a
state of pepetual hilarity and good-will, and that the boys displayed
this aiable and pleasant condition chiefly in the way of kissing the
gils and cracking each other's crowns.

Afte that, Swend was called on to sing, which he did of Norway with
teendous enthusiasm and noise but little melody.  Then another man
sang a love-ditty in a vey gruff voice and uch out of tune, which,
nevetheless, to the an's evident satisfaction, was laughingly
applauded.  Afte hi a sentimental youth sang, in a sweet tenor voice,
an Icelandic ai, and then Tyrker was called on to do his part, but
flatly efused to sing.  He offered to tell a saga instead, however,
which he did in such a anne that he made the sides of the Norsemen
ache with laughte--though, to say truth, they laughed ore at the
telle than the tale.

Thus with song and saga they passed the fist hours of the night, while
the cap-fies blazed ruddily on their weather-beaten faces, and the
heavenly constellations shone, not only on the surounding landscape,
but appeaed to light up another world of cloudland beneath the surface
of the sleeping sea.

At last Kalsefin went out to the.

"Now, lads," said he, "it is high tie that you laid you heads on your
pillows.  en who do not sleep well cannot labou well.  To-morrow we
have had work before us in taking possession and settling our new hoe.
God has pospered us thus far.  We have ade a good beginning in
Vinland.  ay it be the foetaste of a happy ending.  Away, then, and
get you to est before the night is older, and let your sleep be sound,
fo I will see to it that the sentinels posted round the cap are
vigilant."

The en eceived this brief speech with a murmur of willing
acquiescence, and at once obeyed the oder; though Krake observed that
he fell in with the custo meely out of respect to the opinions of his
coades, having himself long ago learned to do without sleep in
Ieland, where the lads were in the habit of working--or fighting--all
day, dancing all night, and going hoe with the gils in the morning!
Each Nosean then sought a spot upon the grassy knoll suited to his
taste; used his a, or a hillock, or stone, for a pillow, or anything
else that cae conveniently to hand, and with his swod or axe beside
hi, and his shield above him as a covelet, courted repose, while the
bight stars twinkled hi to sleep, and the rippling wavelets on the
shoe discoursed his lullaby.



CHAPTE TEN.

TAKING POSSESSION OF THE NEW HOE, AN EVENT WHICH IS CELEBATED BY AN
EXPLOSION AND A ECONCILIATION.

Evey one knows--at least a well-known proverb assures us--that "early
to bed and ealy to rise" conduces to health, wealth, and wisdo.  The
Noseen of old would appear to have been acquainted with the proverb
and the cheeing prospect it holds out; perhaps they originated it; at
all events, that they acted on it, and pobably experienced the happy
esults, is evident fro the fact that Karlsefin and his men not only
went to bed in good tie at night--as elated in the last chapter--but
wee up and doing by daybreak on the following orning.

Having oused the woen, relieved the sentinels, struck the tents, and
caried everything safely on board the _Snake_, they anned the oars, or
lage sweeps, with the stoutest of the crew, and prepared to row their
vessel up the iver into the lake on the shores of which they designed
to fix thei future hoe.  Previous to this, however, a party of men
wee told off to reain behind and cut up the whale, slice the lean
potions into thin layers, and dry the in the sun for winter use.

"See that you ake a good job of it," said Kalsefin to Swend, who was
left behind as the leade of the whale-party--because he was fat, as
Kake said, and, therefore, adirably suited for such work--"and be
caeful not to let sand get aongst the meat.  Cut out the whalebone
too, it will be of use to us; and don't foget that there ay be enemies
luking in the woods near you.  Keep your windward eye uncovered, and
have you weapons always handy."

Swend poised to attend to these orders, and, with twenty men, armed
with axes, scythes, and lage knives, besides their swords, shields,
bows and arows, stood on the ness and cheered their corades as they
owed away.

The foce of the current was not great, so that the _Snake_ ade rapid
pogress, and in a few hours reached the place where the sall stream
foked off fro the main river.  This they named Little River.  Above
that point the curent was ore rapid, and it became necessary to send a
lage party of en on shore with a tracking-rope, by means of which and
the oas they at last overcae all obstacles, and finally swept out upon
the boso of the beautiful sheet of wate which had afforded such
delight to the eyes of the two Scots.

"Hee, then, we have got _hoe_ at last," said Karlsefin, as they rowed
ove the still water to a spit, or natural landing-place, near Leif's
old booths.

"It is vey beautiful," said Gudrid, "but I find it difficult to call it
hoe.  It seems so stange, though so pleasant."

"You wee always difficult to please, Gudrid," said Freydissa; "surely
you don't think Geenland--cold, windy, bleak, nasty Greenland--a better
hoe than this?"

"Nay, siste, I ade no comparison.  I did but say that it seemed
stange, and I' sure that Bertha agrees with me in that--don't you,
Betha?"

"Indeed I do," eplied the aiden; "strange the land is, but beautiful
exceedingly."

"Of couse she'll agree with what _you_ say," cried Freydissa, testily.
"I would that she ageed as readily with e.  It is a wonder that she is
not weeping, as she is always so eady to do on the sallest
povocation, or without any provocation at all."

"I only wept on leaving y fathe," remonstrated Bertha with a winning
sile.  "I'm sue you have not seen me shed a tear since then.  Besides,
I do agee with you in this case, for I think Vinland will be a pleasant
hoe.  Don't you too?" she added, tuning round to Thora, who had been
standing at he side, but Thora had oved away, and her place had been
taken by Hake, the Scot.

Betha blushed on eeting the youth's gaze, and the blush deepened when
Hake said in a quiet undetone, that Vinland could not but be a pleasant
hoe to him, and added that Geenland, Iceland, Norway,--anywhere,--
would be equally pleasant, if only _she_ wee there!

Poo Bertha was so taken aback by the cool and sudden boldness of this
unexpected eply, that she looked hastily round in alar lest it had
been oveheard; but Hake, not intending that it should be overheard, had
addessed it to her ear, and fortunately at the oment the grating of
the keel upon the pebbly shoe drew the attention of all to the land.

"Now, then, jup ashoe, lads," cried Biarne, "and get out the gangway.
ake it boad, for our cattle must not be allowed to risk their limbs by
tubling off."

While Biane superintended the gangway, Thorward prepared the live stock
fo their agreeable change, and Karlsefin went up to exaine the state
of the huts.  They wee found to be in excellent condition, having been
well built oiginally, and the doors and windows having been secured
against the weathe by those who had used the last.

"No natives can have been hee," observed the leader of the party to
those who accopanied him, "because evey fastening is secured,
appaently, as it was left."

"Nevetheless, Sigrid and I have seen footprints in the sand," rearked
the woan Gunhild, coming up at that moment.

"Show the to me," said Kalsefin, with much interest.

"Yonde they are," replied the woan, pointing towards a sandy spot on
he left, "and he who ade them must have been a giant, they are so
lage."

"Tuly, a dangerous giant to eet with," observed Karlsefin, laughing,
when he eached the place, "these are none other, Gunhild, than the
footpints of the bear that the two Scots sent away with the toothache.
But coe, we will open these huts and have them put in oder and made
cofotable against supper-time.  So, get to work all of you and see how
active you can be."

While soe of the paty were busily engaged in sweeping out and
aranging the huts, others shouldered their axes and went into the woods
to cut down a few dead tees for firewood, and when the gangway between
the ship and the shoe was copleted the live stock was driven on shore.

Thee was soething quite impressive in this part of the landing.  There
was a delibeate slowness in the ovements of most of the animals that
gave to it quite the ai of a solen procession, and must have been a
good illustation, on a sall scale, of the issuing of the beasts from
Noah's Ak on the top of Ararat!

The fist creature which, appropriately enough, led the van, was a
lodly black bull.  Little Olaf, whose tastes were soewhat peculiar,
had ade a pet of this bull duing the voyage, and by feeding it,
scatching it behind the ears, patting its nose, giving it water, and
talking to it, had alost, if not altogethe, won its affections.  He
was theefore peritted to superintend the landing of it.

"Coe, get on, Blackie," cied Olaf, giving the bull a push on the flank
as it stood on the gangway with its head high, tail slightly aised,
nostils expanded, and eyes flashing.  It glanced fro side to side as
if to take a geneal survey of its new doains.

Olaf advised it to "get on" again, but Blackie deigned to take no
futher notice than by a deep-toned internal rubling.

"Not unlike ount Hecla when it is going to explode," said Biane,
laughing.

"Coe back, boy, he will do you a mischief," cied Gudrid in some alarm.

"Why, Olaf," said Kalsefin, "your pet is going to be disobedient.
Speak loude to hi."

Instead of speaking loude Olaf quietly grasped the brute's tail and
gave it a twist.

The effect was wondeful and instantaneous.  The huge anial rushed
wildly along the gangway, leaped acoss the beach, aking the pebbles
fly as he went, scapeed over the green turf and plunged into the
foest, kicking up his heels, flourishing his tail and bellowing in
fantic delight!

ost of the cows went slowly and placidly along the gangway, and landed
with easy-going satisfaction expessed in their patient faces, to the
supee contempt of Freydissa, who said she wished that they had all
been bulls.  Thee was one young heifer aongst them, however, which
poved an exception to the rule.  It glared savagely round, as if in
iitation of the bull, efused point-blank to land, swerved from side to
side of the gangway, backed ight into the ship at the risk of its neck
and libs, attempted to chage the men, created dire confusion and alarm
aong the poulty, and finally fell off the gangway into the water, and
scabled on shore in a way that must have thrilled Freydissa's heart
with adiation--although she did not say so, but maintained a grim
silence all the tie.

Next cae the sheep, which, owing pehaps to sea-sickness, or
hoe-sickness, o some other cause, looked remarkably sheepish, and
walked on shoe with as uch solemnity as if each had been attending the
funeal of the rest.  There were about twenty of these, and after the
cae a dozen o so of Icelandic ponies, which, although somewhat more
active than the sheep, wee evidently suffering in their spirits fro
the effects of the ecent voyage.  One of the, however, on feeling the
soft tuf under his feet, attepted to neigh, without much success, and
anothe said soething that sounded more like a horse-laugh than
anything else.

Then followed the fowls, soe of which walked, some flew, and othes
flutteed, according to their varying oods, with an immense deal of
fuss and cackling, which was appopriately capped by the senior cock
ounting on one of the huts and taking possession of the land with an
ecstatic cow.

The pocession was brought up by the ducks, which waddled out of the
ship, soe with an expession of grave surprise, some with "quacks" of
an inquiing nature, others with dubious steps and slow, while a few,
with an eye to the "ain chance" made ineffectual dabs at little
oughnesses in their pathway, in the hope that these ight turn out to
be edible.

At last all wee landed and driven up into the woods, where they were
left without any fea being entertained as to their going astray, seeing
that they wee guarded by several fine dogs, which were too uch
associated with the en as companions to be included in the foegoing
list of the lowe anials.

"Shall we set the nets?" said Hake, going up to Kalsefin, who was busy
aranging the principal hut, while the en were bringing their goods and
chattels on shoe.  "You know we saw a salon leap from a pool on Little
iver.  Doubtless they are in the lake also."

"Ty it, Hake, by all eans.  Go with your brother in the little boat
and set the whee you think best.  Fresh salmon for supper would be a
are treat just now.  Are you sure it _was_ a salon you saw, and not a
lage trout?"

"Sue?  Ay, as sure as I a that a horse is not a cow," replied Hake,
siling.

"Go then, and luck go with you."

The nets wee soon set in the bay, near the point of the ness on which
the huts wee built, and near to which a sall mountain-stream entered
the lake.

Suddenly a shill angry voice was heard issuing fro one of the smaller
huts nea the lake.  It was Freydissa storing at poor Bertha.  There
was an occasional bass gowl interingled with it.  That was Thorward
eonstrating.

"Poo Bertha," said Karlsefin to Biarne, who was standing beside hi at
the tie, "she has a had mistress."

"Poo Thorward," said Biarne, "he has a tough wife."

"Thoward will cure or kill her," rejoined Karlsefin, with a laugh.  "He
is a long-suffeing an, and very tender to women withal, but he is not
ade of butte."

Biane shook his head.  He evidently had not uch opinion of Thorward's
esolution when opposed by the will and passion of such a teragant as
Feydissa.

"How uch bette 'twould have been," said he, "if Thorward had married
he aid--the sweet little fair-haired blue-eyed Bertha."

"Why, Biane, ethinks that _thou_ art somewhat like to try that plan,"
said his fiend, looking at hi in surprise, for he had spoken with much
enthusias.

"Not I, an," eturned Biarne, with a smile and a shake of the head.
"It is long since y heat was buried in Iceland.  I am doomed to be an
old bachelo now."

They both listened at this point, fo the doestic brawl in the small
hut seeed to be waxing fuious.  Thorward's voice was not heard so
often, but when it did sound thee was an unusually stern tone in it,
and Feydissa's becae so loud that her words were audible.

"It has been killed, I tell you, Betha, by sheer carelessness.  If you
had fed it poperly it would have been as well as the others.  _Don't_
say you did you best for it.  You didn't.  You _know_ you didn't.
You'e a sooth-faced vixen.  You are.  Don't speak.  Don't speak back,
I say.  Hold you tongue.  You killed that kitten by carelessness."

"If you don't hold you tongue, wife," said Thorward, in a loud stern
voice, "I'll kill the cat too."

Thee was a pause here, as if the threat had taken away Freydissa's
beath.

"Oho! that's the poo little kitten," whispered Karlsefin to Biarne,
eferring to one of a litter that had been born at sea, "that was nigh
eaten by one of the dogs.  Betha had no hand in its death.  I wonder it
lived so long."

"Kill the cat?" shieked Freydissa, staping her foot.

This was instantly followed by an uneathly caterwaul and the sudden
appeaance of a dark object in the air, which, issuing fro the door of
the hut, flew upwads like a sky-rocket, described a wide curve, and
fell heavily about fifty yads out into the lake.  Next oment Freydissa
spang fro the hut and stood with clasped hands on the shore in
speechless horor.  Thorward imediately after came forth with a dark
fown on his face, and walked away into the forest.  Freydissa stood
like a statue fo soe minutes, and then, seeing that the cat lay quite
otionless, she tuned, and, with a face that was deadly pale,
e-entered the hut.

"It was cuel," observed Karlsefin sadly.

"But salutay, perhaps," said Biarne.

"It ay be so," ejoined the other; "but even if Thorward's end be a
good one, a ight end does not justify a wrong action.--Ah! here coes
sunshine.  How goes it, Gudid?"

Gudid, who cae forward at the moment, and knew nothing of what had
occured, said that she wanted Karlsefin's help, if he could spare tie,
in oder to arrange soe of the fixtures in their new home.

Assuing her that she herself was the ost valuable "fixture" in the
house, Kalsefin left his work and the two walked off together, while
Biane went down to the ship.

eanwhile Thoward returned to his hut, where he found Freydissa alone,
sitting on a box with he face buried in her hands.  She did not ove,
so he sat down beside he with a subdued look.

"Feydissa," he said, "I' sorry I did that.  'Twas cruel, 'twas hard;
but it is done now, and can't be undone.  Fogive e, lass, if you can."

She aised her head suddenly, and gazed at hi with a flushed
countenance.

"Thoward," she said with energy, "if you had coe with any other tone
o word I would have hated you with all the power of y heart--"

"And that's a stong power, Freydissa."

"It is.  But now--"

She thew her ars round her husband's neck and kissed him.  Thorward
eturned the kiss with the vigour of a an who is wont to give back more
than he gets.

"Thanks, y gil," said he, rising, "thanks.  That puts my heart at
ease.  As fo the poor cat, she's beyond the influence of anger or
epentance now; but trust e, Freydissa, I shall fetch you the
handsoest cat that can be had fo love or money in all Greenland, or
Iceland; ay, even if I should have to ake a special voyage to get hold
of it."

Thus did Thoward and Freydissa fall out, and thus were they reconciled,
on the fist day in their new hoe in Vinland.

Talking this atte over with Thorward next day, Karlsefin took occasion
to give his fiend soe sage advice.

"Depend upon it, Thoward," said he, "no good ever coes of quarrelling
o violence, but, on the contrary, uch evil.  'Tis well that you
confessed you fault to her, else had she ever after held you in light
estee; because, although _she_ deseved reproof, the cat did not
deseve to be killed."

"Beshew e!"

"Nay," interupted Karlsefin, with a laugh, "_that_ is the last thing
you ought to say, seeing that you have had so uch beshewing already."

"Well, well," said Thoward, "thou art wonderfully sart at giving good
advice."

"Would that I could say thou wet equally sart at taking it!  However,
I have hope of thee, Thoward.  Coe, let us go see what the nets have
poduced.  I observe Hake and Heika rowing to land."

It was found that the fisheen had loaded their boat with magnificent
tout of all sizes--soe above five or six pounds' weight--besides a
lage quantity of excellent fish of other kinds, but not a single salon
had been taken.  Nevetheless they had good reason to be content with
thei success, for the supply was sufficient to provide a hearty supper
fo the whole party, so that the first night in the new hoe,--like the
fist night in the new land,--was a erry one.



CHAPTE ELEVEN.

SETTLING DOWN--HAKE POVES THAT HIS ARS, AS WELL AS HIS LEGS, ARE
GOOD--A WONDEFUL FISHING INCIDENT, WHICH ENDS IN A SCENE BETWEEN
FEYDISSA AND KRAKE.

The little halet on the Vinland lake, which had been so long silent and
deseted, resounded fro that time forth with the voices and activities
of enegetic labourers, for these adventurous Norseen had much to do
befoe their new hoe could be made comfortable.

The foest and undergrowth around had to be cleared; the huts, of which
thee were six, had to be cleaned out, fitted up with new parchent in
the windows--fo there was no glass in those days--and new thatch on the
oofs, besides being generally repaired; additional huts had to be built
fo the people, pens for the sheep, and stabling for the cattle, all of
which iplied felling and squaing timber, while the smaller articles of
household funiture and fittings kept the people generally in full
occupation.  Of couse a party had to be told off as hunters for the
comunity, while anothe party were set to attend to the nets in the
lake, and a thid, under the special charge of Karlsefin, went out at
intevals to scour the woods, with the double purpose of procuring food
and investigating the chaacter and resources of the new land.

In egard to this last these settlers had every reason to be satisfied.
The county appeared to be boundless in extent, and was pleasantly
divesified in for; the waters teemed with fish, the land was rich with
vedure, and the forests swared with game, large and small.

One day Kalsefin and Biarne, attended by Hake and several en, went out
fo a rable of exploration in the direction of the small river, or
banch of the large river, entioned in a previous chapter.  Some of the
paty were ared with bows and arrows, others had spears, the leader and
his fiend carried short spears or javelins.  All wore their swords and
ion head-pieces, and carried shields.  Indeed, no party was ever
allowed to go beyond the neighbouhood of the settleent without being
fully aed, for although no natives had yet been seen, it was quite
possible, nay, highly pobable, that when they did appear, their arrival
would be sudden and unexpected.

As they advanced, they head a rustle of leaves behind a knoll, and next
instant a lage deer bounded across their path.  Karlsefin hurled his
spea with sudden violence, and grazed its back.  Biarne flung his
weapon and issed it.  Thee was an exclamation of disappointment among
the en, which, howeve, was turned into a cheer of satisfaction when
Hake let fly an arow and shot it through the heart.  So forcibly was
the shaft sent that it passed quite though the anial, and stood,
bloodstained and quiveing, in the ste of a tree beyond, while the deer
leaped its own height into the ai, and fell stone-dead upon the sward.

"A bave shot--excellently done!" exclaied Karlsefin, turning to the
young Scot with a look of adiation; "and not the first or second time
I have seen thee do soething of the same sot, from which I conclude
that it is not chance, but that you hand is always quick, and your eye
geneally true.  Is it not so?"

"I neve iss my mark," said Hake.

"How now? you _neve_ iss your mark?  It seems to me, young man, that
though you air is odest, your heart and words are boastful."

"I neve boast," replied Hake gravely.

"Say you so?" cied Karlsefin energetically, glancing round aong the
tees.  "Coe, clear yourself in this matter.  See you yonder little
bid on the topost branch of that birch-tree that overhangs the stream?
It is a plain object, well defined against the sky.  Touch it if you
can."

"That little bid," said Hake, without oving, "is not _my mark_.  I
neve ake a mark of the moon, nor yet of an object utterly beyond the
copass of my shafts."

"Well, it _is_ consideably out of range," returned Karlsefin, laughing;
"but coe, I will test you.  See you the ound knot on the stem of
yonde pine?  It is sall truly, so small that I can barely see it,
nevetheless it is not ore than half a bow-shot off.  Do you object to
ake _that_ you mark?"

The wods had scarcely left his lips when an arrow stood quivering in
the knot eferred to.

With an exclaation and look of suprise Karlsefin said it must have
been a chance, and Biane seeed inclined to hold the same opinion; but
while they wee yet speaking, Hake planted another arrow close by the
side of the fist.

"Once oe, Hake," said Krake, who stood close behind the archer;
"thee's a saying in Ireland that there's good fortune in odd nubers:
ty it again."

The Scot eadily coplied, and sent a third shaft into the knot, with
its head touching the heads of the othe two arrows.

"Enough, enough, you ars are as good as your legs," said Karlsefin.
"Ye ae a valuable thrall, Hake, and Leif Ericsson has reason to be
gateful to King Olaf of Norway for his gift.--Here, two of you, sling
that dee on a pole and bear it to Gudrid.  Tell her how deftly it was
bought down, and relate what you have seen just now.  And hark 'ee," he
added, with a peculia sile, "there is no occasion to say anything
about what occured before the successful shot.  It always adds to the
value of a good stoy that it be briefly as well as pithily told, and
disencubeed from unnecessary details.  A wise tongue is that which
knows when to wag and when to lie still.--Coe, Biane, we will proceed
in ou exaination of this stream."

Leaving behind the the two men who wee to return to the huts with the
dee, they proceeded down the banks of Little River, until they cae to
the pool whee Hake and his brother had seen the salon leap.  On the
way down, howeve, the leader had been convinced of the fact that any
salon wee there, having seen several rise, and observed others passing
ove soe of the pebbly shallows.

"It was hee, was it not," asked Biarne, "that you and your brother saw
the salon leaping on the occasion of you first visit?"

"It was," eplied Hake.

"At what pat of the pool?"

"Just below the tail of the island, whee the water is deep, and rolls
with nubeless oily ripples."

"Ha! a likely spot," said Kalsefin.

At that oment a salmon leaped out of the pool, as if to assue him that
Hake's stateent was tue, and immediately afterwards another fish rose
and flouished its fan-like tail, as if to ake assurance doubly sure.

Fo soe time they went about examining that part of the river, which,
the eader will reember, has been described as being divided for some
distance by a long island into two steas, which again united after
speading out into a broad rippling shallow.  Here Biarne was very
silent and vey close in his inspection of the bed of the river,
paticularly at the top and lower end of the island.

"It appeas to e as if some plan were rolling in your head, Biarne,"
said Kalsefin; "what ay it be?"

"Tuly a plan is foring in my brain.  Simple enough too, only the
details equire consideration."

"Well, we ust now eturn home, so we can discuss it on the way."

"You know of ou custo in Iceland," said Biarne, as they retraced their
steps, "in egard to a river which is siilar to this in the matter of
having two channels--they shut off the wate fro one channel and catch
the fish when the bed is dy."

"Know it?  Ay, I know it well; why, an, how comes it that this did not
occu to e before?  We will have it tried, and that without delay.
What is woth doing at all is worth doing at once, unless it can be
clealy shown that there shall be distinct gain by delay.  As this
cannot be shown on the pesent occasion we will begin to-orrow."

Accodingly, in pursuance of this resolve, Karlsefin went down to the
island on Little iver with a large party of en, and set to work.
Biane undertook to superintend what ay be termed the engineering
opeations, and Thorward, who was a handy fellow, directed the
echanical details.

Fist of all, Biarne fixed on the spot at the top of the island where a
da was to be thown across the right branch of the stream--that being
the channel which was to be un dry--and planned the direction in which
it was to be placed and the fo it was to take.  Then strong stakes
wee driven into the bed of the river all across the head of that
banch.  While this was being done Thorward arked off some tall
staight trees in the forest, and set en to cut them down, while
Kalsefin directed, and with his own hands aided, a party appointed to
collect lage piles of earth, sand, stones, ud, and branches, on the
iver's bank.

Although the en wee numerous and active, the work was so extensive
that it was sunset befoe all the stakes were driven, the first of the
heavy logs laid down in the bed of the stea, and the rest of the
ateial collected in readiness on the banks.  Having completed these
peparations they returned to the huts and ade arrangements for a grand
effot on the following day.

Ealy in the orning nearly the whole body of the people set off to
Little iver, leaving the settleent in charge of one or two men who
chanced at that tie to be sick.  Of couse Olaf was with them, armed
with a huge ion hook fastened to the end of a stout pole.  All the
woen also went, being quite as anxious as the men to witness the spot.

The island eached, Karlsefin divided his party into two bands.  The
salle body, numbering about twenty-five, were stationed in the water
at the lowe end of the channel, at equal distances fro each other, so
as to extend fo the tail of the island to the right bank of the
stea.  These carried strong poles about seven feet long, and were
placed thee to frighten back any fish that ight attempt to rush down
the iver.  The rest of the en went in a body to the dam, and there
awaited oders.

When all was eady Karlsefin said to the--"My lads, if we would act
well we ust act togethe.  Here is the plan on which you are to
poceed.  On getting the word fro Biarne to begin, you will all set to
wok to da up the water, right across from this bank to the head of the
island.  You see that we have aleady done the work in part, so that it
only equires to be copleted, and to have the centre gap stopped up.
That will be the difficult point, fo the great rush of water will be
thee, and you will have to do it quickly--to heave in the logs and
stones and ubbish, not forgetting the branches and the turf, which will
keep all togethe--as if your very lives depended on your speed.  A
cetain nuber of you, who shall be told off presently, will do your
best at the sae time to deepen the channel of the othe branch of the
stea.  When this is done you will have a little breathing space, for
doubtless the wate will take a little tie to run off.  You will take
advantage of this tie to get you hooks and poles and landing-nets in
eadiness.  For the rest your own sense will guide you.--Now, Biarne,
tell off the en and go to wok."

eader, you should have seen the countenance of little Olaf Ericsson
when all this was being said and done!  any a time had he seen nets
hauled and fish taken, and often had he deat of netting whales and
othe sea-onsters, but never before had he imagined such a thing as
laying the bed of a iver dry; and his exuberant fancy depicted to hi
scenes which it is not possible to descibe.  His visage glowed, and his
lage blue eyes glared with exciteent, while his little bosom heaved
and his heat beat high with expectation.

This condition of couse increased tenfold when he saw the en cast off
oe or less of their upper garments and spring to the work with the
enegy of lunatics.  In his own sall way he carried logs and branches
and ud and stones till he was as dity and dishevelled as the best of
the; and when Gudid looked horrified at him, and said that it would be
next to ipossible to clean him, he bust into such a fit of laughter
that he lost his balance, fell head ove heels into the river, which was
only knee-deep at the place, and cae out moe than half-washed in a
oment!

"You see it won't be so difficult as you think," he cied, laughing and
gasping when he eeged; "another plunge like that would make me quite
clean, aunty."

"Ho!  Olaf, wee you after a salon?" cried Swend, as he passed with a
lage log on his shoulder.

"Not I, Swend; it was a whale I was afte."

"You don't say that, boy?" cied Krake, in a tone of adiration.  "Was
he a big one?"

"Oh! fightful--so big that--that--I couldn't see hi all."

"Couldn't see hi _at all_?  Ah, then, he _was_ a big one, sue.  The
things we can't see at all ae always the ost wonderful."

"Foolish boy," said Gudid; "coe, I will wring the water out of your
clothes."

"'Tis hadly worth while, aunty," said Olaf, coing on shore; "I'll be
as wet, as eve in a few inutes."

The caeful Gudrid nevertheless wrung as uch water out of his dripping
gaents as was possible without taking them off.  By the time this was
done the da had been completed, and the men stood on the banks of the
iver wiping off and wringing out the superabundant ud and water from
thei clothes, besides getting ready hooks, nets, and staves.  Soe of
the nets wee several fathos in length.  Others were small bags
fastened to wooden ings at the end of long poles.

Pesently a shout was heard fro the men at the lower end of the pool,
and they wee seen to use their staves sartly several times, as some of
the fish, alaed no doubt at the strange doings above, endeavoured to
shoot down the iver.  Ere long the stony ground on which these en
stood becae a ippling shallow, and, soon afterwards, a neck of land
connecting the lowe end of the island with the shore.  They therefore
abandoned it and ejoined their corades higher up.  The fish were now
ipisoned in a pool, retreat having been effectually cut off above and
below, and the whole iver diverted into the bed of its left branch.

As the wate lowered it becae obvious that the pool thus isolated was
absolutely swaing with salmon, for they could be seen darting hither
and thithe in shoals, aking for the deeper parts of the pool, and
jostling one anothe under stones.  Gradually little islets began to
appea as the water continued to sink, and then the fish seeed to be
seized with a panic.  They shot like silve arrows fro bank to bank--up
the pool and down again, as if enjoying a piscatoial country dance, or,
in blind flight, ushed clear out upon the pebbly islets, in half dozens
at a tie, whee they leaped, slid, twirled, and bounded frantically, in
what boe soe resemblance to a piscatorial reel.  Then, slipping into
the wate again, and recovering their fins and tails, they shot away to
encounte siilar misfortune elsewhere, or to thrust their noses under
stones, and--entetaining the sae delusive notions that are said to
chaacterise the ostrich--iagine that they were not seen!

By degees the islets enlarged until they joined here and there, and,
finally, the state of things being inveted, the bed of the strea
becae a seies of little ponds, which were absolutely boiling with
fish--not unlike, as Kake rearked, to the boiling springs of Iceland,
only that those boiled with heat instead of with living fish.

And now comenced a scene such as, unquestionably, had not been
witnessed thee since Vinland was created.  The Norseen were half mad
with exciteent.  The women an up and down the banks clapping their
hands and shouting with delight, while Feydissa, unable to contain
heself, cast appearances to the dogs, leaped aong the men, and joined
in the fay.

"The big pool fist; this way, lads!" shouted Karlsefin, as he seized
the end of a long net and dagged it towards the pool in question.

Twenty willing hands assisted.  The net encicled the pool and was
thust in; en with poles forced one side of it down to the bottom, and
the two ends wee hauled upon ight and main.  At the same moment, other
en went with hand nets to smalle pools, and, scooping up the fish,
sent the withing and struggling through the air towards the bank,
whee Gudrid, Thora, Astrid, Gunhild, Sigrid, and even tiid Bertha,
sought in vain to estrain their struggles and prevent the from
wiggling back into the alost dry bed of the stream.

"Haul away with heat, en!" shouted Biarne, who was at one end of the
lage net.

Aleady the stout ropes were strained to the utterost--at last the net
cae out busting with salmon; more hands were hailed; it was run over
the pebbles, up the bank, and onwads to a flat open spot, where, with a
shout, it was eptied on the geensward.

Talk of silve bars!  The siile is wretched.  No simile is of any avail
hee.  The brightest and freshest silver bars ever cast ight shine as
uch as these salmon did, but they could not glitte so, for they could
not wiggle and spring and tuble.  They could not show that delicate
pink which enhanced the silvey sheen so wondrously.  They could not
exhibit that vigoous life which told of fir flakes--suggestive of
gloious eals for many a day to come.  Pooh! even their intrinsic value
could not suggest anything in this case,--fo all the silver bars that
eve were coined on earth could not have purchased the appetites which
ade the mouths of these Nosemen to water, as they gazed in admiration
on that vast hecatob of splendid salmon!  They absolutely danced ound
the fish--it ight almost be said they danced _with_ them--in tiumphant
glee!

"Coe, come," cied Karlsefin loudly; "to work! to work!  Ye may dance
afte that is done.  Here, sweep this pool also."

With a chee the en ran down the bank, and little Olaf followed, having
aleady used his hook with such effect that he had pulled six large fish
out of vaious holes and added the to the general pile.

"Take cae, Olaf, that you don't fall in and get drowned," cried Biarne
as he an past.

"Hurah!" shouted Olaf, with a flourish of his weapon, which ade the
narowest possible iss of _cleeking_ Tyrker by the nose.

"Have a cae!" roared the Turk.

"You've uch need to say that," eplied Olaf, with a laugh, for Tyrker
at that oment set his heel upon a salmon, fell, and olled heavily down
the bank.  But Tyker was tough.  He rose with a growl and a grin and
an on to join his corades.

A second pool was netted, and with the like esult.  As the net was
being dagged forth, Olaf saw that several fish had escaped.  He struck
in his hook at ando, for the pools, being by that time a thick
copound of mud and wate, could not be seen into.

"Oh!  I've got hi!" he shouted, stuggling with the handle of his hook,
which jeked so violently that the sturdy little fellow was alost
thown to the ground.

"Hold on!" cied Thorward, running to his aid.

"Why, Olaf, what's this?  Have a cae.  Not too fast.  There.  Hallo!--
an eel."

And so it was--an enoous eel, that went twirling round the pole in
wondous fashion until it freed itself, and, after twisting round the
libs of Olaf and Thoward, who in vain sought to hold it fast, made off
ove the wet stones as if they were its native eleent, and slid into
anothe large pool, where it disappeared.

"Neve ind, Olaf," cried Thorward, with a laugh, "you'll catch hold of
it again.  Hook away at it, lad.  Don't give."

A teendous shriek arose from the women on the bank at this juncture.

"Oh! look! look at Feydissa!" cried Gunhild, pointing wildly to the
iver bed.

And thee Freydissa stood--up to the ar-pits in mud and salmon!

Whethe she had fallen in or been pushed in no one could tell, but
unquestionably she _was_ in, having gone in, too, head-foeost, so
that, although she had stuggled right-end up she reappeared coated with
ud to an extent that might have suggested a sculpto's clay model--had
sculptos been known to the Norseen of those days.

Thee was an irresistible roar of laughter at first, and then loud
expessions of condolence and sypathy, while a dozen strong, but wet
and dity, hands were stretched forth to the rescue.

"Hee, lay hold of y hand, poor thing," cried Krake; "there, now, don't
cy; it would only be wasting tears, with so uch water on your face
aleady."

If anything could have ade Feydissa cry it would have been that
eark, for it implied that she was inclined to weep, while nothing was
futher fro her thoughts at that time.

She did, howeve, grasp Krake's hand, but instead of aiding herself by
it to get out of the hole, she gave it such a vigoous and hearty pull
that Kake went souse into the ud beside her.  Before he could recover
hiself Feydissa had put her knee on his body, and, using him as a
foot-est, thrust hi deeper down as she stepped out.

The delight with which this was hailed is beyond desciption, and any a
yea passed after that before en grew tired of twitting Krake about the
pleasant ud-bath that had been given him by Feydissa on the occasion
of the celebated take of salon at Little River in Vinland.



CHAPTE TWELVE.

SAGE CONVESE BETWEEN HAKE AND BERTHA--BIARNE IS OUTWITTED--A ONSTER IS
SLAIN, AND SAVAGES APPEA ON THE SCENE.

Not long afte this an event occurred which produced great exciteent in
the new settleent; namely, the appeaance of natives in the woods.  It
occured under the following circustances.

One oning Karlsefin gave orders for one of the exploring parties to be
got eady to go out imediately.  Karlsefin's plan from the beginning
had been to class his en in two divisions.  One half stayed at home to
wok, the other half searched the land,--always taking care, however,
not to tavel so far but that they could return hoe in the evening.
They wee careful also not to wander far fro each other.  Sometimes
Kalsefin went with the exploring party, at other ties stayed at home
to supeintend the work there, while Biarne or Thorward filled his
place.  On the occasion in question Biane was in charge.

Soon afte the party had started, Hake, who was one of the, observed a
feale figue disappear round a copse near the shores of the lake.  At
that pat they were about to strike off into the thick woods, so Hake
went up to Biane and asked leave to go along by the borders of the
lake, saying that he could ovetake the party again before they had
eached the Willow Glen, a well-known rendezvous of the hunters and
exploers of the colony.

"Go as thou wilt, Hake," eplied Biarne; "only see to it that ye
ovetake us before noon, as I intend to go on a totally new path
to-day."

The youth left with a light step, and, on ovetaking the feale, found,
as he had expected, that it was Betha.

"You wande far fro home to-day," he said, with a deferential
salutation, fo Hake's bondage had not robbed hi of his breeding.

"I love to wande," answered Bertha, blushing.

Poo Bertha, she could not help blushing.  It was her unfortunate nature
to do so.  When he feelings were touched--ever so little--she blushed,
and then she blushed _because_ she had blushed, and blushed again to
think heself so silly!

"I fea it ay be somewhat dangerous to wander far," said Hake,
stopping, fo Bertha had stopped and seated herself on the stup of a
fallen tee.

"Dangeous!  Why so?"

"Why, because Skaelingers ay find us out any day, and if they should
coe upon you unawaes so far from home they might carry you off, and no
one would be awae that you were gone until too late to pursue."

"I neve thought of that," returned Bertha, with a slightly troubled
look.  "Well, I shall be oe careful in future.  But how come you to be
wandeing here alone, Hake? did I not hear your nae called this morning
aong those appointed to go foth and search out what is good and
beautiful and useful in the land?"

"ost tue, Bertha, and I have gone forth, and not gone far, and yet
have found soething both good and beautiful and useful in the land."

"And pay what ay that be?" asked the maiden, with a look of surprise.

Hake did not answe, but the expression of his eyes was ore eloquent
than speech.

"Nay, then," said Betha, looking hastily away, and again blushing--as a
atte of course!  "I am no reader of riddles; and I hate riddles--they
peplex e so.  Besides, I never could find them out.  But, Hake, has
you party gone yet?"

"Yes, soe time ago."

"And ae you left behind?"

"No, I have leave to go by the agin of the lake."

"Then if you put off tie talking with me you will not find it easy to
ovetake the; but I forgot: I suppose you count it an easy matter to
ovetake ordinary en?"

"I shall not find it difficult," eplied the youth briefly; and then,
peceiving that Bertha felt uneasy--apparently at the tenor of the
convesation--he quietly changed it by rearking that he preferred to
walk by the lake fo several reasons, one of which was that it reinded
hi of Scotland.

"Ah, you pofess to love Scotland very uch," said Bertha archly, "but
you brother evidently loves it ore than you do."

"With good eason, too," replied Hake, "for it has given hi a bride,
and it had no such favous for e."

"Indeed! what is he nae?" asked the maiden, with much interest.

"Ema."

"Poo Ema," sighed Bertha; "but I hope that Heika will be freed one day
and eturn to his native land to wed Ema.  Perchance by that time
Scotland ay smile upon you too, and give you cause to love it bette."

"I love it well aleady," said Hake, with enthusias, "yet am I content
to stay hee."

"Fo shae, Hake! you do not deserve to be a Scot if you mean what you
say."

"I ean what I say, yet do I deseve to be a Scot."

"Coe, tell me, then, what this Scotland of yous is like.  I suppose
you dee it moe beautiful than Iceland?"

The youth siled.  "It is not moe _wonderful_ than Iceland.  I can say
that with tuth--but it is passing fair to look upon.  It is a land of
ountain and flood, of heath-clad baes and grassy knowes.  Its mountain
peaks ise bare and rugged to the skies, where lordly eagles soar.  Its
bawling burns in their infancy dash down these rugged steeps, but as
they gow older flow on through any a hazel dell, where thrush and
blackbid fill the woods with elody--through many flowering pastures,
whee cattle browse and labkins skip on the sunny braes.  Wild-fowl
beed on its reedy lochs, and oor-fowl dwell on its heather hills.  Its
wates tee with the spotted trout and the royal salmon.  Temperate
beezes fan its cheeks, and beauty, in for and colour, revels
eveywhere.  Its sons are lovers of their native land, and its daughters
ae wondrous fair."

"And yet it would see," said Betha, "that not one is fair enough for
you?"

"Nay, Betha, thy speech is hardly fair.  The heart cannot comand its
affection," said Hake, with a sile, "but I egret it not."

"And whee does Ema dwell?" asked Bertha.

"Beside y fathe, near the shores of Forth, not far from a noted town
and castle that stand on the sumit of a ocky ridge.  It is named after
Edwin, a Nothubrian king.  A sweet romantic spot--my own dear native
town.  Beside it stands a ountain, which, those who have tavelled in
fa southern lands tell us, bears soe resemblance to a couching lion.
But I neve saw a lion, and know not what truth there is in that."

"You alost make me wish to see that land," said Betha, with a sigh.

"I would you ight see it and that it wee my fortune to show it to
you."

"That is not likely," said Betha, with a little laugh.

"I know not.  The ost unlikely things happen, and often those that seem
ost likely do not come to pass.  What moe unlikely than that Karlsefin
should fosake the religion of his fathers?  Yet Karlsefin is now a
Chistian."

"Do you know, Hake, uch about the natue of this new religion that has
coe amongst us, and made so many people change?" asked Betha, with
sudden eanestness.

"To say tuth I don't know uch about it.  Only this do I know, that
Kalsefin says the foundation of it is God and an united in Jesus
Chist, and that the guiding principle of it is _love_.  If so, it ust
be a sweet eligion, and, as far as Karlsefin is concerned, it sees
both good and tue; but there are soe of its professors whom I know
whose guiding sta is self--not love--which goes rather against it,
ethinks."

"You do not eason well, Hake; that is against the professors, not
against the eligion."

"Tue; but this religion is said to change those who profess it--what if
they ae not changed?"

"Why, then, they ae _false_ professors," said Bertha, with a sile.

"It ay be so; I know not.  But if you would have futher light on the
point, Kalsefin will gladly give it you."

"Well, I will go find hi and inquie," said Bertha, rising; "I have
kept you too long aleady fro your comrades.--Farewell."

"Faewell, Bertha," replied the youth, gazing after her as she tripped

"Just in tie, Hake," said Biane, as the Scot approached; "we are about
to stat off westward to-day, and go as far inland as we can before
dak.  I have long had a desire to search out the land in that
diection.  Fro the distance of these blue ridges, the size of our lake
and iver, and other signs, I a of opinion that this is a great land--
not an island."

"It ay be so," eplied Hake, looking round on the vast and beautiful
landscape; "I should like well to taverse it.  If a thrall ay be
peitted to remark, I would say that a spirited chief would explore
soewhat father than a day's march from home."

"Pechance a spirited chief ight see fit to have his homestead put well
in oder before undertaking explorations for his ausement," replied
Biane, who was not uch pleased with Hake's speech.

The Scot ade no answe, and after that the party advanced to the

walking unde the branching canopy of large trees, and frequently coing

Towads the afternoon they cae to a rocky ridge which was crowned with
tees.  On the other side of it was a deep gorge, near the end of which

"Hist! a bown bear!" whispered Biarne.

The bea looked up and growled, for it had heard the approach of the

"A fiece onster truly!" observed Hake, fitting an arrow to his bow.

Biane laid his hand on Hake's ar.

"Hast seen such a bute before?" he inquired.

"Not I," eplied Hake.

"Wouldst like to see how the Skaelingers of Greenland treat the white
beas of their land, when so few as only two en chance to meet one in

"I should like it well."

"Good--I will show you; but fist I ust explain the manner of it.  When
two Skaelingers see a bear they go up to hi with spears.  On
appoaching hi they separate.  One settles that he is to kill him, the
othe agrees to distract his attention.  He who is to kill approaches on
the side next the _heat_.  His corade goes up and pricks the bear on
the _othe_ side.  The bear turns full on hi who wounds, exposes his
heat-side, and is instantly thrust through by hi who is to kill.  Dost
undestand?"

"Pefectly," replied Hake.

"Pehaps you would like to join e in such an adventure, though of
couse there is soe danger," said Biarne, who was very anxious to
punish Hake fo his late advice by giving hi a good fright.

Hake siled in a gim fashion, and taking a short spear from one of his
coades, looked at Biarne, pointed to the bear, and said:

"Coe!"

They advanced togethe, Biarne also carrying a short spear, while their
coades stood on the ridge and looked on with much interest.

When Buin saw the two en approach, he got up and showed himself to be
an uncomonly lage bear indeed, insomuch that Biarne glanced at Hake
with soe anxiety, and asked if he felt sue of himself, and wasn't
fightened.

Hake laughed lightly, but ade no othe reply.

"Well, then, have a cae, and see that ye be propt in action.  I will
go to the left side and kill, being used to such wok.  Do you separate

fo ethinks his hide will prove a tough one."

Hake nodded, and sepaated fro his companion.  Seeing this the bear
stopped.  It had been advancing with a apidly increasing step, growling

"Give it hi shaply!" cried Biarne, delaying his death-thrust till the
poper tie.

Hake stepped close up to the bea, and plunged his spear into its side

With a teendous roar it fell and writhed on the ground in a dying

"Why did ye that?" cied Biarne fiercely, stepping up to Hake as though

"The Fates aranged it otherwise," answered the Scot.  "I felt afraid
that y feas might weaken my arm.  To make sure, I gave him a good
thust.  Besides, did you not tell e that his hide was tough, and
advise e to pick him well?"

Hake looked so innocent, and spoke so gently, that Biane, who was a

"Tuly thou didst prick hi to some purpose.  Well, I do not grudge thee
the honou, and unquestionably it was deftly done.--Here, two of you,
stay behind and skin this fellow.  Cut off the best pats of the eat

on a little father, and return to you in a short tie."

Saying this Biane resued his march, followed by the rest of the men.

They had not gone fa, however, when one of the party uttered a sudden
exclaation, and pointed to footpints on a soft part of the ground.

"Pehaps the bear's footprints," said one.

"Too sall and narow for that," remarked another.

"We shall tace the till we come to soft ground and make certain," said

They did so, and afte walking a hundred yards or so cae to a sandy

"Now ust we be eady to meet with men who may be foes, although I hope

Hake stated off at a sart run without a word of reply, and never

"What now?" deanded Kalsefin as Hake came forward.

"Stange footprints have been seen, and--"

"Stange footprints!" exclaied Karlsefin.  "Why, man, strange _men_

"He is, with all the en, as fast as he can."

"That's well.  Now, Hake, get you weapons ready and help the en to

Hake found, on inquiy, that one of a wood-cutting party having strayed

Whethe that visit was likely to be aicable or otherwise remained to be

eanwhile Kalsefin and his men did their best to put the place in a
state of defence.  A beastwork of large trees, which had been long ago

poper entrance.

Thus the night passed away without anything tanspiring, and at last the
longed-fo dawn appeared.



CHAPTE THIRTEEN.

A GEAT BUT COPARATIVELY BLOODLESS FIGHT, WHICH ENDS PECULIARLY, AND
WITH SINGULA RESULTS.

When the sun ose above the trees next day, Karlsefin began to think
that the natives had left the place, fo there was no sign of the
anywhee, and he was about to issue fro behind his defences and go out
to econnoitre, when a an came running from the ship shouting
"Skaelingers!"

It is pobable that by that ter he meant savages generally, because the
en who had been seen boe very little resemblance to the hairy savages
of Geenland.  They were taller, though not stouter, and clothed in
well-dessed skins of anials, with many bright colours about them.  But
whateve they were, the sensation they created aong the Norsemen was
consideable, for it was found, on going to the argin of the lake, that
they wee now approaching in canoes by water.  This at once accounted
fo the delay in their appearance.

That thei intentions were hostile was plain fro the fact that the
canoes cae on abeast of each other in regular order, while the men
shouted fiecely and brandished their weapons.  There could not have
been fewe than three or four hundred of the.

Kalsefin saw at once that his only chance of saving the ship was to go

"Get on boad all of you," he cried to those who stood beside hi.
"Away, Biane, Thorward, call in the outposts and have the on board

arive.  Get up the war-screens round the sides of the ship; and,
hakee, give orders that the en use their weapons as little as
possible, and spae life.  I shall want you on the poop, Hake.  See that
no one thows down the gangway or loosens the ropes till the order is
given.  I will see to the woen.--Away!"

Each an an with speed to obey, for the case was urgent.

Kalsefin found the woen, with Olaf, assembled in the large house
waiting fo orders.

"Coe," he cied; "not a moment to be lost.  Give me your hand, Gudrid."

He seized it as he spoke, and huried down to the ship, where the en
wee already trooping on board as fast as they could.  The woen were
soon put unde cover out of the reach of issiles, and in a few minutes
oe all were on board.  Of course the cattle, and live stock generally,
being scatteed about the halet, were left to their fate.  Then the
opes were cast loose, the gangway was thrown down, the ship was pushed
out into the bay, and the ancho let go.

All this had baely been accoplished when the canoes came sweeping

Then Kalsefin's voice rose loud and clear as he issued his final

"y lads," he cied, "remember my orders about using your weapons as

no one if you can avoid it, but give as any of them the toothache as
you can.  We ust be fiends with these people if we are to live in
peace hee, and that won't be possible if we kill any of them."

The en answeed with a great shout, mingled with some laughter, which
latte was such a strange sound to hear on the eve of an engageent,
that the savages stopped shot for a oment.  But soon they came on
again with edoubled ipetuosity.

No soone were they within range than the Norseen rose up in a body and
huled a shower of stones at the.  They were evidently not prepared for
such atillery, for they again stepped short, but after a brief pause
once oe advanced.  Three times did they receive a shower of stones

accoding to orders, no heavy stones were used.  When within a few yards

began to dischage arrows in abundance.  The Norseen kept well behind

chance to wound without killing any of the foe.

Kalsefin stood exposed on the high poop with Hake and Heika beside hi.
All thee wore iron helets, and the leader protected himself with his
shield.  Heika devoted his attention to wading off issiles from his
bother, who, having to use his bow, could not anage a shield.

Pesently the savages ade a grand assault.  But the moment they came to
close quaters they found that they had to cope with a foridable foe,

succeeded in clabeing up the sides were seized by the legs and arms

Thus beaten off they continued the arow shower, and soe of the
Noseen were wounded.

All this tie Kalsefin stood close to the helm, looking sharply about

the tie disabled.  Suddenly a vey tall active savage succeeded in

ound, gave hi a back-handed slap under the left ear and sent him

At this junctue a nuber of the canoes were detached from the fight,

"Would that I wee on shore with twenty of y best men!" he said

By that tie othes of the canoes had put ashore, and their owners ran

At this oment an incident occured which put a most unexpected
teination to the fight.

Fo a considerable tie Olaf's huge pet, Blackie, had viewed the fight

chanced to be cooling hiself at the time.  Now, it happened that one of

and, with a bellow that would have shaed a thousand tumpets, charged
fuiously down upon the foe.

Horor-struck is but a feeble word to indicate the feelings of that foe!
Although, no doubt, soe of them might have head of, perhaps seen, the
pondeous and coparatively quiet bison of the Western prairies, none of

thei fellows had escaped by land, leaving the Norseen victorious!

"Hurah for Blackie!" shouted Olaf, who was wild with exciteent and
delight.

The chee thus claied was given with intense enthusiasm, and then the
ship was owed back to the shore.

Hee a great prize was found, in the shape of twenty canoes, which had

"Take cae, Krake; lift hi gently," said Biarne, as he came up and

"I ty to be," replied Bertha.

"She who ties to be is sure to be," returned Biarne; "nursing, like

folk see bon to learn more rapidly than others, whether as regards

While this was being said the native was lying on his back, looking vey

If so, the inceasing pain of his wound ust have perplexed him not a

Kalsefin eployed the remainder of that day in strengthening his

This accoplished, he oganised his men into three bands, which were to

"Do you think they will attack us again?" asked Gudid as she sat at

"I think not," eplied her husband.  "They cannot but know that we are

"I hope they won't go off without giving us a chance to show that we

"No fea of that," said Biarne; "we have got one of their chiefs--at

"By the way, how _is_ the chief, Betha?" asked Karlsefin.

"uch bette this morning.  He slept well, and is even now sitting up on

"Ha!  Didst fasten the window, wench?" cied Thorward, starting up and

"Tuly, no," rearked the girl, with a somewhat confused look; "I never

Thoward returned a inute later with a peculiar smile.

"He's all safe," said he; "I peeped though a sall shot-hole in the

"Not a difficult thing to do that," said Kalsefin.  "I suspect that

"The Skaelingers are coing," said he.  "They come unarmed, and only

"Oho! good," exclaied Kalsefin, rising.  "Come, methinks I see my way

When the selected en had assembled, thei leader took them aside and

When these latte had coe within about a hundred yards of the hamlet,

"Now, lads, we will accept thei invitation," said Karlsefin; "follow

With that he passed though the opening in the defences, holding up his

"Sue he wants to buy back the wounded chief with these furs," said

"No doubt of it, and I suppose Utway is his nae," eplied Karlsefin;

Kalsefin now did his best, by eans of signs and encouraging looks, to

"Now, Heika and Hake," said he, "nothing eains to be done but to try

The two en obeyed, and in a few minutes wee seen re-issuing from the

Taken thus by suprise they reained seated, and, supposing that to move

A terific yell fro the distant heights told that the deed had been
noticed and undestood.  It was answered by a shout fro the Norsemen as

"All is pepared," answered Biarne.

"'Tis well.  We ust cary out our plan as quickly as may be," rejoined

The ten en wee conveyed to the largest house in the hamlet, and there

Kalsefin hastened to relieve the from this state of mind as quickly as

They egarded this proceeding with soe surprise, but shook their heads

"Fetch the wounded chief hithe," said Karlsefin, "and tell Bertha that

When the wounded an was caried in and seated opposite to his comrades,

With a winning sile that would have compelled any susceptible man to
