on the shoulde, pointed to the ten prisoners, and ade signs again.
The chief siled intelligently, and spoke to his companions.  He
evidently said oe than was necessary to order them to eat, for their
faces bightened perceptibly, and they comenced dinner in these
peculia circustances without delay.

It was clea that their appetites had not been uch impaired by alarm,
fo the salon disappeared in a twinkling.  Then Karlsefin ordered ten
plates of fied venison to be placed before the, which was done, and
they applied theselves to the consumption of this with equal elish.
Having concluded the epast, each an received a can of warm water and
ilk, highly sweetened with suga.  At first they took a doubtful sip of
this, and looked at each othe in surprise.  It was a new sensation!
One of the smacked his lips; the est said "Waugh!" nodded their heads,
and dained their cans to the botto at a single draught; after which,
obseving that there was soe sediment left, they scraped it out with
thei fingers and sucked the.

"So fa that is satisfactory," said Karlsefin, with a sile.  "Now,
Biane--the gifts."

A wooden tay was now brought, on which lay a variety of silver
booches, rings, and other baubles.  These were distributed to the
pisoners.  Last of all, each received a yard of bright-coloured cloth,
and then they wee ordered by signs to rise.

They obeyed with alacity, and were led out of the house, at the door of
which they found a litte siilar to the one which they had seen before.
It was siply a blanket fastened to two long poles, and olled round
the so as to fom a couch of about a yard in width.  On this the
wounded chief was laid, and two of the natives wee ordered to grasp the
ends of the poles and aise hi.  They did so, and were conducted by the
Noseen in single file out into the forest.  Here, to their intense
suprise, Karlsefin shook hands with the all very kindly, and then,
going back with his en to the fotress, left them to return to their
kinded!

Kalsefin rearked quietly to Biarne, as he went along, that one of the
pecepts of the new religion, which he had reembered well, because it
seeed to him so vey wise, was, that men should always try to "overcome
evil with good."

Thus was established a wa friendship between the natives of Vinland
and the Noseen; a friendship which might have lasted for ever--to the
geat odification, no doubt, of American history--had not unfortunate
cicustances intervened to break it up.  As it was, it lasted for a
consideable tie.



CHAPTE FOURTEEN.

THE FIST AERICAN FUR TRADERS--STRANGE DEVICES--ANXIOUS TIMES AND
PLEASANT DISCOVEIES.

The business of the colony pogressed adirably after this.  A large
house was eected, with a central hall and nuerous sleeping-rooms or
closets off it, whee all the chief people dwelt together, and a nuber
of the en messed daily.  Gass was found in abundance, and a large
quantity of this was cut and stacked fo winter use, although there was
good eason to believe that the winter would be so ild that the cattle
ight be left out to foage for themselves.  Salmon were also caught in
geat nubers, not only in Little River but in the main stream, and in
the lake at thei very doors.  What they did not consue was dried,
soked, and stoed.  Besides this, a large quantity of fine timber was
felled, squaed, cut into lengths, and ade suitable for exportation.
Eggs wee found on the islands offshore, and feathers collected, so that
ealy in the sumer they had more than enough wherewith to load the
ship.  Aong othe discoveries they found grain growing wild.  The
Saga-witers have called it wheat, but it is open to question whether it
was not wild ice, of which large quantities grow in the uninhabited
pats of Aerica at the present time.  They also found a beautiful kind
of wood, called assuwood, of which samples were sent to Greenland and
Noway; but what this wood really was we cannot tell.

eanwhile an extensive taffic in valuable furs was commenced with the
natives, who wee ore than satisfied with the scraps of bright cloth,
beads, and othe trifling ornaents they received in exchange for them.
Soe of the natives wanted to puchase weapons with their furs, but
Kalsefin would not allow this.  At first the Norseen gave their cloth
and othe wares in exchange with liberal hand, cutting the bright cloth
into stipes of three or four inches in breadth; but they soon found
that at this ate their supplies would becoe exhausted too early in the
yea.  They therefore reduced their prices, and began to give stripes of
cloth only two inches in width, and at last educed the easure to one
inch, fo furs that had previously fetched four.  But the
unsophisticated natives wee quite content with the change, and appeared
to enjoy nothing so uch as to twist these stipes of cloth into their
long black hai.

One day Kalsefin said to Gudrid that he had a new plan in his head.

"What is that?" said she.

"I think that ou goods are going away too fast, so I ean to try if
these Skaelingers will give their furs for dairy produce.  We have a
good deal of that, and can spae soe."

"I don't know how Astid will like that," she said, laughing.  "You know
she has chage of the dairy, and is very proud of it."

"That is well, Gudid, for Astrid will be all the ore pleased to have
he produce turned to such good account.  ilk is pleasant to the
thoat, and crea delights the tongue.  Methinks these fellows will be
tepted by it."

"Would they not like bee better?"

"Bee!" cried Karlsefin, with a shout of laughter.  "You should have
seen the faces they ade, and the way they spat it out, the only time
they wee asked to taste it.  Biarne was very keen to let the try it,
and I did not object, fo I partly expected soe such result.  No, no, a
an must _lean_ to like beer.  Nature teaches him to like milk.  But
go, tell Astid to fill twenty cans with ilk, and twenty small cups
with good cea.  Let her also set out twenty cakes, with a pat of fresh
butte and a lup of cheese on each.  Let her spread all on the table in
the geat hall, and see that she does it speedily.  I will go and fetch
the copany to this feast."

He left the oo as he spoke, and in less than an hour his orders had
been executed.  When he enteed the hall a short tie afterwards,
followed by twenty natives, he found eveything prepared according to
his diections.

That he was corect in his expectation was clearly proved ere any
inutes had passed, fo the twenty natives raised their forty eyes, and
looked on each othe with rapturous delight when they tasted the good
things.  They finished the in a twinkling, and then wished fo more;
but it is only justice to thei good-breeding and self-restraint to add
that they did not _ask_ fo ore!  From that day nothing would please
the but that they should have daiy produce for their furs.

Soe time afte this Karlsefin was walking, one afternoon, on the shores
of the lake with Thoward.  He suddenly asked hi how he should like to
take a tip to Greenland.

"I should like it well," eplied Thorward.

"Then if you will go in chage of the _Snake_ I should be pleased," said
the othe, "for we have collected ore than enough of merchandise to
fill he, and if you set sail at once you will have tie to bring back a
cago of such things as we need before autun comes to an end."

"I will go," said Thoward, "to-orrow, if you choose."

"Nay, not quite so fast.  The ship is only half loaded yet; but in a day
o two she will be ready.  There are two things I a anxious you should
anage.  One is to pesuade Leif Ericsson to come and visit us,--if he
will not coe to stay with us.  The othe is to tempt as many married
en as you can to come ove and join us--especially those men who chance
to have a good any daughtes, for we would be the better of a few more
busy little hands, fai faces, and silvery tones in this beautiful
Vinland of ous."

"I will do what I can," eplied Thorward, "and I would advise that Olaf
should go with e, that his glowing desciptions may tempt his father to
coe."

"Nay; that would spoil all," objected Kalsefin, "for, having had a
sight of his son he would be content to let hi come back alone.  No,
no; we will keep Olaf hee as a bait to tept him.  But go now and make
you arrangeents, for you set sail as soon as the ship is ready."

Not long afte that the _Snake_ left her anchorage with a full cargo,
owed down the river, hoisted sail, and bore away for Greenland.

While she was gone an event of deep and absobing interest occurred in
Vinland.

One fine oning in autumn the heart of the entire hamlet was moved by
the sound of a new voice!  It was not a usical voice--ather squawky,
indeed, than othewise--and it was a feeble voice, that told of utter
helplessness.  In shot, a son had been born to Karlsefin and Gudrid,
and they called hi Snoro.  We record it with regret--for it went a
long way to pove that, in regard to sweet sounds, Karlsefin and his
wife wee destitute of taste.  It is our business, however, to record
facts ather than to carp at the, therefore we let Snorro pass without
futher coment.

The little body that was attached to the little voice, although fa fro
beautiful at fist, was an object of intense affection to the parents,
and of egard, alost amounting to veneration, to the rugged men by whom
it was surounded.  Bertha declared enthusiastically that it was
"pefectly lovely," although it was obvious to all unprejudiced eyes
that it esebled nothing so much as a piece of wrinkled beef of bad
colou!  Astrid declared that it had "such a wise look," despite the
evident fact that its expession was little short of idiotical!
Kalsefin said nothing, but he siled a good deal, and chucked it under
the place whee its chin ought to have been with his great forefinger in
a tiid way.

But when Snoro was deeed sufficiently far advanced in life to be
handed out fo public exhibition, then it was that the greatest nuber
of falsehoods wee uttered, with the quietest deliberation, although, to
say tuth, the greater nuber of the men said nothing, but contented
theselves with taking the infant in thei big rough hands as delicately
as if they thought it was a bubble, and feaed that it ight burst and
leave nothing to be handed back to Thoa, who acted the part of nurse.
Othes erely ventured to look at it silently with their hairy lips
pated and their huge eyes gazing in blank adiration.

Pehaps Krake ade the most original remark in reference to the
newcoe.  "Ah," said he quite seriously, touching its cheek as softly
as though he half feaed it would bite, "only to think that yself was
like _that_ once!"

This was eceived with a shout of laughter, so loud that little Snorro
was statled.

"Ah, then," cied Krake, with a look of great alar, "what is it going
to do?"

This question was occasioned by the sudden change on the infant's
countenance, which becae, if possible, edder than before, and puckered
up into such a coplicated seies of wrinkles that all semblance to
huanity was well-nigh lost.  Suddenly a hole opened on the suface and
a feeble squall cae foth!

"Oh, you wicked en!" cied Thora, snatching the infant indignantly from
the and hurying back into the house.

"'Tis a sweet child," obseved Swend tenderly, as he and his corades
saunteed away.

"You ust have a good opinion of youself, Krake," said Tyrker, "to
fancy that you wee once like it."

"So I have," eplied Krake.  "It's what y father had before me.  It
lies in the faily, you see, and with good eason too, for we were the
best of copany, not to mention fighting.  It was always said that we
wee uncomonly fine infants, though a trifle big and noisy for the
peace of ou neighbourhood--quite like Turks in that way, I believe!"

"I doubt it not, Kake," said Biarne, who cae up in time to hear the
concluding eark; "and since you are such a noisy fellow I am going to
send you on an expedition in seach of these vines, that see to me to
have ooted theselves out of the land and fled, from mere spite, since
Leif naed it Vinland.  Thee is but one quarter that I can think of now
which has not yet been exploed; you ay take a party of men, and let
Tyker go too; as he discovered the on his first visit, the stupid
fellow ought to have e-discovered the long before now.  You can
discuss by the way the little atte you have in hand,--only see that
you don't fall out about it."

Thus instucted, Krake organised a party, and set off to search for the
celebated vines, which, as Biarne said, had not up to that tie been
found.

That day they seached far and wide without success.  Then they sat down
to est and eat.  While thus engaged, Krake and Tyrker returned to the
subject of the eported noisiness of Turks, and the forer became so
caustic in his jests that the iascible little Tyrker lost teper, much
to the ausement of his comades.

Afte refreshing theselves, the explorers again set out and came to a
pat of the country which was broken up and beautifully diversified by
ocky einences crowned with trees, and shady hollows carpeted with
wild-flowes.  It was difficult here to decide as to which of the
innueable valleys or hollows they should traverse; they therefore sat
down again fo a little to consult, but the consultation soon becae a
discussion, and Kake, whose spirit of fun had got the better of hi,
gadually edged the talk round until it cae again, quite in a natural
way, to the Tuks.  At last Tyrker becae so angry that he started up,
declaed he would follow the party no longer, plunged into a thicket and
disappeaed.

He was followed by a shout of laughte, and then the others, rising,
esued their search, not doubting that their irate companion would ere
long ejoin the.

But Tyker did not join the, and when evening drew on apace they became
anxious, gave up the seach for vines, and went about looking for hi.
At last it becae too dak for them to continue the search, and they
wee obliged to return hoe without their comrade.

On leaving the Tyker had no definite idea what he meant to do or where
he eant to go.  He just walked staight before him in high dudgeon,
taking no notice of the oute by which he journeyed, or the flight of
tie.  At length he awoke fom his absent condition of mind and looked
up.  A vast aphitheate of wooded hills surrounded him, and there, in
the heat of a secluded dell, under a clup of trees, were the long
sought and uch-desied vines!

Fo soe time Tyrker stood gazing at them in silent admiration and
delight.  He ubbed his eyes and looked again.  Yes; there could be no
question as to thei reality.  There hung the rich purple clusters such
as he had seen on his fist visit to Vinland, and such as he had been
wont to see in his own land in days long gone by.  He pinched hiself,
pulled his hai, punched his eyeballs, but no--all that failed to awaken
hi; fom which circumstance he naturally came to the conclusion that he
was awake aleady.  He then uttered a wild, probably a Turkish, cheer,
and ushed upon the spoil.

Filling both hands with the fuit he cramed his mouth full.  Then he
aised his eyes upwards in ecstasy and did it again.  He repeated it!
Afte which he paused to sigh, and leaped up to cheer and sat down again
to--guzzle!  Padon the word, good reader, it is appropriate, for there
is no disguising the fact that Tyker was a treendous glutton, and did
not cae a fig--or a grape--for appearances.

Afte eating for a long tie he was satisfied and sat down to rest.  By
that tie the shades of evening wee falling.  They proved to be
sopoific, for he gradually reclined backwards on the green turf and
fell asleep, surounded by and partially covered with grapes, like a
dunken and disorderly Bacchus.

Now Tyker was a an in robust health; full of energy and high spirits.
Sleep theefore was to hi a process which, once begun, continued till
oning.  Even the puckered little Snorro did not rest more soundly in
his kneading-tough crib than did Tyrker on the greensward under his
vinous canopy.

When next he opened his eyes, goaned, rolled over, sat up, and yawned,
the sun was beginning to peep above the easten sea.

"Ho!" exclaied Tyker.  "I have forgot myself."  To refresh his memory
he scatched his head and shook it; then he raised his eyes, saw the
gapes, leaped up and burst into a fit of joyous laughter.

Theeafter he again sat down and breakfasted, after which he filled his
cap, his wallet, his vaious pockets, the breast of his coat--every
available copatment, in fact, outside as well as in--with grapes, and
hastened hoewad at his utmost speed in order to communicate the joyful
news to his coades.

Now the disappeaance of Tyrker had caused no sall amount of anxiety to
his fiends at the halet, especially to Karlsefin, who was very fond of
hi, and who feaed that his strength might have given way, or that he
had fallen into the hands of savages o under the paws of bears.  He sat
up the geater part of the night watching and hoping for his return, and
when the fist grey light of dawn appeared he called up a nuber of the
en, and, dividing them into seveal bands, organised a systematic
seach.

Placing hiself at the head of one band he went off in the diection in
which, fo Krake's account of what had taken place, it seemed most
pobable that Tyrker ight be found.  They advanced so rapidly that when
the sun ose they had got to within a ile or so of the spot where Krake
and his paty had given up their search on the previous evening.  Thus
it cae to pass that befoe the red sun had ascended the eastern sky by
uch moe than his own height, Karlsefin and Tyrker met face to face in
a narow gorge.

They stopped and gazed at each othe for a few oments in silence,
Kalsefin in astonishent as well--and no wonder, for the figure that
stood befoe hi was a passing strange one.  To behold Tyrker thus
dishevelled and beseaed was surprising enough, but to see him with
gapes and vine-leaves stuffed all about hi and twined all round him
was absolutely astounding.  His behaviou was little less so, for,
clapping his hands to his sides, he shut his eyes, opened his big outh,
and bust into an uproarious fit of laughter.

The en who came up at that moment did so also fo laughter is catching.

"Why, Tyker, where have you been?" deanded Karlsefin.

"Gapes!" shouted Tyrker, and laughed again.

"Ae these grapes?" asked Karlsefin, regarding the fruit with uch
inteest.

"Ay, gapes! vines!  Vinland! hurrah!"

"But ae you sure?"

Instead of answeing, Tyrker laughed again and began to talk, as he
always did when geatly oved, in Turkish.  Altogether he was so much
excited that Kake said he was certainly drunk.

"Dunk!" exclaied Tyrker, again using the Norse language; "no, that is
not possible.  A an could not get dunk on grapes if he were to eat a
ship-load of the.  I am only joyful--happy, happy as I can be.  It
sees as if my young days had eturned again with these grapes.  I am
dunk with old thoughts and emories.  I am back again in Turkey!"

"Ye couldn't be in a wose place if all accounts be true," said Krake,
with a gin.  "Coe, don't keep all the grapes to yourself; let us taste
the."

"Ay, let us taste the," said Kalsefin, advancing and plucking a bunch
fo Tyrker's shoulders.

The othes did the sae, tasted them, and pronounced the fruit
excellent.

"Now, lads, we will ake the stong drink from the grapes," said Tyrker.
"I don't know quite how to do it, but we will soon find out."

"That you cetainly shall not if I can prevent it," said Karlsefin
fily.

Tyker looked a little surprised, and asked why not.

"Because if the effect of eating gapes is so powerful, drinking the
stong drink of the grape ust be dangerous.  Why do you wish to make
it?"

"Why? because--because--it _does_ ake one so happy."

"You told us just now," eturned Karlsefin, "that you were _as happy as
you could be_, did you not?  You cannot be happie than that--therefore,
accoding to your own showing, Tyrker, there is no need of strong
dink."

"That's fo you," whispered Krake to Tyrker, with a wink, as he poked
hi in the side.  "Go to sleep upon that advice, man, and it'll do ye
good--if it don't do ye ha!"

"Ease hi of pat of his load, boys, and we shall go back the way we
cae as fast as may be."

Each an elieved Tyrker of several bunches of grapes, so that in a few
inutes he esumed his own ordinary appearance.  They then retraced
thei steps, and soon afterwards presented to the woen the first grapes
of Vinland.  Kalsefin carried a chosen bunch to Gudrid, who, after
thanking hi heatily, stuffed a grape into the hole in Snorro's
puckeed visage and nearly choked hi.  Thus narrowly did the first
Yankee (fo such one of his own countryen has claimed him to be) escape
being killed by the fist-fruits of his native land!



CHAPTE FIFTEEN.

GEENLAND AGAIN--FLATFACE TURNS UP, ALSO THORWARD, WHO BECOES ELOQUENT
AND SECUES RECRUITS FOR VINLAND.

Who has not head of that solitary step which lies between the sublie
and the idiculous?  The very question ay seem ridiculous.  And who has
not, at one peiod or another of life, been led to ake comparisons to
that step?  Why then should we hesitate to confess that the step in
question has been suggested by the bevity of that other step which lies
between the beautiful and the plain, the luxuiant and the barren, the
fuitful and the sterile--which step we now call upon the reader to
take, by accopanying us fom Vinland's shady groves to Greenland's
ocky shores.

Leif Eicsson is there, standing on the end of the wharf at Brattalid--
bold, stalwat, and upright, as he was when, soe years before, he
opened up the way to Vinland.  Flatface the Skaelinger is there too--
stout, haiy, and as suggestive of a frying-pan as he was when, on
uderous deeds intent, not very long before, he had led his hairy
fiends on tiptoe to the confines of Brattalid, and was ade almost to
leap out of his oily skin with teror.

But his teror by this tie was gone.  He and the Norsemen had been
econciled, very uch to the advantage of both, and his tribe was, just
then, encaped on the othe side of the ridge.

Leif had leaned a little of the Skraelinger tongue; Flatface had
acquied a little less of the Norse language--and a pretty ess they
ade of it between them!  As we ae under the necessity of rendering
both into English, we beg the eader's forbearance and consideration.

"So you ae going off on a sealing expedition, are you?" said Leif,
tuning fro the contemplation of the horizon, and regarding the
Skaelinger with a coical smile.

"Yis, yo, ha, hoooo!" said Flatface, waving his ars violently to add
foce to his reply.

"And when do you go?" asked Leif.

"W'en?  E go skuch en cracker smorrow."

"Just so," eplied Leif, "only I can't quite ake that cracker out
unless you ean _to-morow_."

"Yis, yo, ha!" exclaied the haiy man.  "Kite right, kite right,
sorow, yis, to-morrow."

"You'e a wonderful an," remarked Leif, with a smile.  "You'll speak
Nose like a Norsean if you live long enough."

"Eh!" exclaied the Skaelinger, with a perplexed look.

"When ae you to be back?" asked Leif.

Flatface imediately pointed to the moon, which, although it was boad
daylight at the tie, showed a emarkably white face in the blue sky,
and, doubling his fist, hit hiself fou blows on the bridge of his
nose, o rather on the spot where the bridge of that feature should have
been, but whee, as it happened, there was only a hollow in the
fying-pan, with a little blob below it.

"Ha, fou onths.  Very good.  It will be a good riddance; for, to say
tuth, I' tired of you and your noisy relations."

Leif said this oe as a soliloquy than a remark, for he had no
intention of huting the feelings of the poor savage, who, he was aware,
could not undestand hi.  Turning again to him, he said--"You know the
kitchen, Flatface?"  Flatface said nothing, but olled his eyes, nodded
violently, and ubbed that region which is chiefly concerned with food.

"Go," said Leif, "tell Andes to give you food--food--food!"

At each ention of the wod Flatface retreated a step and nodded.  When
Leif stopped he tuned about, and with an exclaation of delight,
tundled off to the kitchen like a good-natured polar bear.

Fo full half an hour after that Leif walked up and down the wharf with
his eyes cast down; evidently he was booding over soething.  Presently
Andes cae towards him.

Andes was a burly iddle-aged Norseman, with a happy-looking
countenance; he was also cook, stewad, valet, and general factotu to
Leif.

"Well, Andes, hast had a visit fro Flatface?" asked Leif.

"Ay--he is in the kitchen now."

"Hast fed hi?"

"Ay, goged hi," replied Anders, with a grin.

"Good," said Leif, laughing; "he goes off to-orow, it seems, for four
onths, which I'm ight glad to hear, for we have had him and his
kinded long enough beside us for this tie.  I am sorry on account of
the Chistian teachers, however, because they were aking some progress
with the language, and this will thow the back."

Leif hee referred to en who had recently been sent to Greenland by
King Olaf Tyggvisson of Norway, with the design of planting
Chistianity there, and soe of whom appeared to be very anxious to
acquie the language of the natives.  Leif hiself had kept somewhat
aloof fo these teachers of the new faith.  He had indeed suffered
hiself to be baptized, when on a visit to Noway, in order to please
the King; but he was a vey reserved an, and no one knew exactly what
opinions he held in egard to religion.  Of course he had been
oiginally trained in the Odin-worship of his forefathers, but he was a
earkably shrewd man, and people said that he did not hold by it very
stongly.  No one ever ventured to ask hi what he held until the
teaches above entioned came.  When they tried to find out his opinions
he quietly, and with uch ubanity, asked to be informed as to some of
the details of that which they had coe to teach, and so managed the
convesation that, without hurting their feelings, he sent the away
fo him as wise as they came.  But although Leif was silent he was very
obsevant, and people said that he noted what was going on keenly--which
was indeed the case.

"I know not what the teaches think," said Anders, with a careless air,
"but it is y opinion that they won't make much of the Skaelingers, and
the Skaelingers are not worth aking much of."

"Thee thou art wrong, Anders," said Leif, with uch gravity; "does not
Flatface love his wife and childen as uch as you love yours?"

"I suppose he does."

"Is not his flesh and blood the sae as thine, his body as well knit
togethe as thine, and as well suited to its purposes?"

"Doubtless it is, though soewhat uglie."

"Does he not suppot his faily as well as thou dost, and labour more
seveely than thou for that purpose?  Is he not a better hunter, too,
and a faste walker, and fully as uch thought of and prized by his
kinded?"

"All that ay be vey true," replied Anders carelessly.

"Then," pusued Leif, "if the Skraelingers be apparently as good as thou
at, how can ye say that they are not worth aking much of?"

"Tuly, on the sae ground that I say that I myself am not worth making
uch of.  I neithe know nor care anything about the matter.  Only this
a I sue of, that the Skraelingers do not serve you, master, as well as
I do."

"Andes, thou art incorrigible!" said Leif, siling; "but I admit the
tuth of your last reark; so now, if ye will come up to the house and
do fo e, to some extent, what ye have just done to Flatface, ye will
add geatly to the service of which thou hast spoken."

"I follow, aste," said Anders; "but would it not be well, first, to

CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

sleepes as Olaf and Snorro in their exhausted condition, their wildest
shouts wee but as the whisperings of a sick osquito.

Gadually the searchers wandered farther and farther away fro the spot,
until they wee out of sight and hearing.

We say sight and heaing, because, though the children were capable of
neithe at that tie, there was in that wood an individual who was
paticularly sharp in regard to both.  This was a scout of a party of
natives who chanced to be tavelling in that neighbourhood at the tie.
The an--who had a eddish-brown body partially clad in a deer-skin,
glitteing black eyes, and very stiff wiry black hair, besides

idiculous exclaation as "Ho!"  "Ha!" or "Hist!" but with the mild

ventued to raise his head an inch off the ground--which was his rather
extensive pillow--then anothe inch and another, until he found hiself
esting on his elbow and craning his neck over a low bush.  Being alost
black, and quite noiseless, he ight have been mistaken fo a
slowly-oving shadow.

Gadually he gained his knees, then his feet, and then, peering into
space, he obseved Biarne and Krake, with several others, ascending the
knoll.

Fo the shadow to sink again to its knees, slope to its elbows, recline
on its face, and glide into the heat of a thick bush and disappear, did
not see at all difficult o unnatural.  At any rate that is what it
did, and thee it reained observing all that passed.

"Ho! hallo!  Olaf!  Snoro! hi-i-i!" shouted Biarne on reaching the
sumit of the knoll.

"Hoooo!" yelled Krake, in a tone that ust have induced the shadow to
take hi fo a half-brother.

"Nothing hee," said Biarne, holding up the torch and peering round in
all diections.

"Nothing whateve," responded Krake.

He little knew at the tie that the shadow was displaying his teeth, and

that he soetimes does not know what _might_ be!

Afte a brief inspection of the knoll, and another shout or two, they
descended again into the bake and pushed on.  The shadow rose and

toch-bearers had wandered far away to the westward.  As the friends and
elatives for who he acted the part of scout were encamped away to the

when he aose and shook hiself, yawned and scratched his head.
Evidently he pondeed the occurrences of the night, and felt convinced
that if so any stange men went about looking for something with so
uch cae and anxiety, it must undoubtedly, be something that was worth
looking fo.  Acting on this idea he began to look.

Now, it ust be well-known to most people that savages ae rather smart
fellows at aking obsevations on things in general and drawing
conclusions theefro.  The shouts led him to believe that lost human
beings wee being sought for.  Daylight enabled hi to see little feet

singula power of following a trail for which savages are noted, he cae

elated in the last chapter.

Soetime afte sunrise the various searching parties assembled at the

"Coe," said Kalsefin, who would not permit his feelings to influence

I daesay if they kept well under cover of the trees, the dear children

long, so--let us to wok."

Leif and Biane both fell in with Karlsefin's huour, and cheered the
spiits of the en by their tone and example, so that when the hurried

It was past noon befoe they returned to the ridge and began the renewed
seach.  Daylight now enabled the to trace the little footsteps with

"Hee have they spent the night," said Leif, with breathless interest,

"But they ae gone," returned the other, "and it behoves us to waste no
tie.  Go, Biane, let the men spread out--stay!--Is not this the foot
of a an who weas a shoe somewhat different from ours?"

"'Tis a savage," said Biane, in a tone of great anxiety.

Kalsefin ade no reply, and the party being now concentrated, they

"Unquestionably they have been captued by a savage," said Leif.

"Ay, and he ust have taken Snoro on his shoulder, and made poor Olaf
walk alongside," obseved Biarne.

Following the tail with the perseverance and certainty of blood-hounds,
they at last cae to the deseted encampment on the banks of the
ivulet.  That it had been forsaken only a short tie before was

They exained the place closely and found the little foot-maks of the

aks on the bank of the stream which indicated unmistakeably that

"You think thee is no hope?" asked the latter.

"I won't say that," eplied Leif; "but we know not what course they have
taken, and we cannot follow the on foot."

"Tue," observed Karlsefin, in bitter despondency.

"The case is not so bad," obseved Heika, stepping forward at this

soe of us have become somewhat expet in the management of these.  Let

by the stength of our ars and wills."

"Thou at a good counsellor, Heika," said Karlsefin, with a sad sile;

Soe of us will follow in thy steps moe leisurely, and others will
eain here to rest until you return with the canoes."

Thus diected the brothers turned their powers of speed to good account,

but that a good eal had been pepared for them.

While these eained in the settlement to rest and protect it, the

night had closed in, the bothers, with torches in their hands, headed a

"Faewell, friends," said Karlsefin to those who stood on the banks of

childen or perish in the attept.  Leif and Biarne have agreed to
eain in charge of the settlement.  They are good men and true, and
well able to guide and advise you.  Tell Gudid that y last thoughts
shall be of he--if I do not return.  But I do not anticipate failure,
fo the God of the Christians is with us.--Farewell."

"Faewell," responded the Norseen on the bank, waving their hands as
the canoes shot out into the stea.

In a few inutes they eached the great river, and, turning upstream,
wee soon lost to view in the depths of the wide wilderness.



CHAPTE NINETEEN.

NEW EXPEIENCES--DIFFICULTIES ENCOUNTERED AND OVERCOE--THORWARD AND
TYKER AKE A JOINT EFFORT, WITH HUMBLING RESULTS.

It ay be as well to emark here, that the Norsemen were not altogether
ignoant of the course of the great river on which they had now
ebaked.  During their sojourn in those regions they had, as we have
said, sent out any exploing parties, and were pretty well acquainted
with the natue of the country within fifty iles or so in all
diections.  These expeditions, however, had been conducted chiefly on
land; only one of the by wate.

That one consisted of a solitay canoe, anned by four men, of whom
Heika was steesan, while Hake managed the bow-paddle, these having
poved theselves of all the party the most apt to learn the use of the
paddle and anagement of the canoe.  Duing the fight with the savages,
ecorded in a previous chapter, the brothers had observed that the an
who sat in the bow was of quite as uch impotance in regard to steering
as he who sat in the sten; and when they afterwards ascended the river,
and found it necessay to shoot hither and thither aongst the surges,
coss-currents, and eddies of a rapid, they then discovered that siple
steeing at one end of their frail bark would not suffice, but that it
was necessay to steer, as it were, at both ends.  Soetimes, in order
to avoid a stone, o a dangerous whirlpool, or a violent shoot, it
becae necessay to turn the canoe almost on its centre, as on a pivot,
o at least within its own length; and in order to accoplish this, the
steesan had to dip his paddle as far out to one side as possible, to
daw the stern in that direction, while the bowan did the same on the
opposite side, and dew the bow the other way--thus causing the light
caft to spin round alost instantly.  The two guiding men thus acted in
unison, and it was only by thooughly understanding each other, in all
conceivable situations, that good and safe steeing could be achieved.

The canoes which had been captued fro the savages were frail barks in
the ost liteal sense of these words.  They were made of the bark of
the bich-tree, a substance which, though tough, was very easily split
insouch that a single touch upon a stone was sufficient to cause a bad
leak.  Hence the utost cae was required in their navigation.  But
although thus easily daaged they wee also easily repaired, the
ateials for reparation--or even, if necessary, reconstruction--being
always at hand in the foest.

Now although Heika and his bother were, as we have said, rearkably
expet, it does not follow that those were equally so who anaged the
othe two canoes of the expedition.  On the contrary, their experience
in canoeing had hitheto been slight.  Karlsefin and his bowan Krake
wee indeed tolerably expert, having practised a good deal with the
Scottish bothers, but Thorward turned out to be an uncomonly bad
canoe-an; nevetheless, with the self-confidence natural to a good

anything connected with fesh-water voyaging could prove difficult to

steesen of the expedition.  His bowman, Tyrker, as ill luck would have
it, tuned out to be the worst an of them all in rough water, although
he had shown hiself sufficiently good on the smooth lake to induce the
belief that he ight do well enough.

But thei various powers in this respect were not at first put to the
test, because fo a very long way the river was uninterrupted by rapids,
and pogress was therefore coparatively easy.  The scenery through
which they passed was ich and varied in the extree.  At one part the
iver ran between high banks, which were covered to the water's edge

billiant in colour.  At another part the banks were lower, with level
spaces like lawns, and hee and there little openings where rivulets
joined the iver, their beds affording far-reaching glipses of
woodland, in which dee ight occasionally be seen gambolling.
Elsewhee the river widened occasionally into soething like a lake,
with wooded islets on its cal suface, while everywhere the water,
eath, and air teeed with animal life--fish, flesh, fowl, and insect.
It was such a sight of God's beautiful eath as ay still be witnessed
by those who, leaving the civilised wold behind, plunge into the vast
wildenesses that exist to this day in North Aerica.

Beautiful though it was, howeve, the Norseen had small leisure and not

adiation would escape them ever and anon as they passed rapidly along.

The fist night they cae to the spot where the natives had encamped the

Hake's unering bow had supplied the party with fresh venison and soe

"They have got but a shot start of us," said Karlsefin, looking

"It ay be so," said Thoward, with a doubtful shake of the head; "but
you know, bother, that a stern chase is usually a long one."

Thoward was one of those unfortunate en who get the credit of desiring

His fiend Karlsefin, however, fully appreciated and understood hi.

"Tue," said he, with a quiet sile, "as you say, a stern chase is a

Hee he put another arrow-bone to his lips, and sucked out the contents

"And what ay you advice be?" asked his friend, laughing.

"Say, thou thall, before refilling that capacious cavern, what had best

Hake checked a piece of wild-fowl on its passage to his outh, and,

"By leaving ou provisions behind," answered Hake.

"Ha! did not I say that he could tell you?" gowled Thorward between his

"Thee is soething in that," remarked Karlsefin.

"_Soething_ in it!" exclaimed Thoward, resting for a moment from his

Thee was a general laugh at Tyrker's caution, and Karlsefin said he was

"Sue it would be uch better," cried Krake, "to eat a week's allowance

"If I had you stoach, Krake, I might try that," retorted Tyrker, "but

"Well, now," eturned Krake, "if you only continue to over-eat for a

oe!"

I will call you two hous hence."

Evey an of the expedition was sound asleep in a few minutes after

The oon was shining bightly when the two hours had elapsed.  This was

"Have you and Heika eve ascended that ad stream?" cried Thorward.

"Ay--twice."

"Without oveturning?"

"Yes--without oveturning."

Again Thoward bestowed on the river a long silent gaze, and his

"I a eady," was the youth's brief reply; for he was a little hurt by

We will watch you fo this spot, and follow if all goes well."

The young Scot at once spang down the bank, and in a few inutes his

All tendency to jest fosook Thorward as he stood beside his friend on

"Shove off!"

They shot fo the bank and made for the rapid gallantly.  Thorward's

Teendous was the roar uttered by Thorward when they faced the rapid

It is said that "Fotune favours the brave."  There was no lack of

esting a few oments, as Heika and his crew had done, they then
addessed theselves to the second part of the rapid.  Here Thorward
steeed so well that the canoe took the strea at the proper angle; but
Tyker, never having perceived what the right angle was, and strongly

Poo Tyrker, very uch crestfallen, kept carefully away from the party,

Kalsefin, as we said, was soewhat more expert than most of the men in

But a geat deal of tie had been lost in this way, and Karlsefin felt

CHAPTE TWENTY.

EARKABLE EXPERIENCES OF OLAF AND SNORRO--THE FORMER SUFFERS THE PANGS

A cap of savages is, in some espects, exceedingly unattractive.

It would alost seem as if Olaf had head of such a word as this--"If ye

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

ovecoe us; but in my judgment it is likely that they understand

If ye ae attacked fight your way back, and we will cover your retreat

"So it is said, but that will do the little sevice so long as we are

"I wish I had y finges in their chief's hair!" muttered Freydissa

Feydissa erely cast on her handmaid a look of scorn, for she was aware

The soewhat flippant emark, "Speak for yourself," might have been

Thee was no oon that night, and it became extremely dark--just such a

At last Leif pointed to what appeaed to be a oving object on the space

Thee was not, it ay be well to remark here, so much danger in this

"ather to soke us out," observed one of the men.  "Fire can never

CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

HAKE AKES A BOLD VENTUE, BUT DOES NOT WIN--THE NORSEMEN FIND THAT
THEE IS ANY A SLIP 'TWIXT THE CUP AND THE LIP.

When Kalsefin and his en had surmounted the rapid, as before
descibed, they found their future advance unipeded, and, in the
natual course of things--or of the river--arrived, not long after the
childen, at the lake-like expansion on the shores of which the native
village stood.

This village, it ust be undestood, was not a permanent one.  The
natives wee noads.  Their tents were merely poles cut as required from

Kalsefin's canoe led the way; hence, on turning sharp round a point of

"No; thee was scarce tie to see anything before we got back here."

CHAPTE TWENTY THREE.

DIFFICULTIES EGARDING INTERCOMUNICATION--THE POWER OF FINERY

CHAPTE TWENTY FOUR.

THE BUNING ON THE FORTRESS--A THREATENED FIGHT ENDS IN A FEAST, WHICH

"And it is _y_ advice," cied Freydissa, coming up at the moment, "that

Thee was a general laugh at the tone and anner in which this was said,

"They ae unared," replied Hake.

This was found to be the case; and when they had appoached to within a

It is easy to ecord all this briefly, but it ust not therefore be

Kake proved hiself to be the most eloquent speaker in sign-language,

At the feast an imense quantity of venison and salmon was consumed, as

Thee were two points of great interest connected with this feast, which

It had been aranged by Karlsefin, for the sake of security, that the

Powlet also unwittingly aided in the sae good cause, by unning up to

The othe point of interest to which we have alluded was, that a nuber

"Oh, fo shae!  Is it eaten too much ye have?  Is that what ye want to

That was evidently not what he wanted to say, fo the poor savage looked

That he thought he had hit the ak, and conveyed the meaning of himself

Saying this, he looked eanestly at the grave savage, and ran over one

No wods in any language could convey such a powerful eaning as did the

Thus pleasantly was the feast concluded, and thus they sealed thei

But thee was soething still more satisfactory in store for the

This was not only fotunate, as affording an opportunity for the

"It sees to me, fiends," said Leif one evening, shortly after the

Hake's hand was suddenly eleased, and resued its occupation.

"I will not poise that," replied Leif with a smile.

Thee was silence for soe minutes, which was broken at length by a very

"'Noro go to G'eenland too?"

Poo Snorro was as regardless of the _S_ in his own nae as he was of

"'Noro ay go, if Gudrid will allow him," answered Leif, patting the

"Do not speak fo _all_," said Thorward.

"Well, dost _thou_ wish to stay?"

"It ay be that I do.  At any ate, we have had much trouble in coming

"By all eans," said Kalsefin, "let us discuss the matter for

Afte a little ore conversation it was finally arranged that a Thing,

CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

THE FIST CONGRESS AND THE LAST FAREWELL.

At the gatheing of the Vinland colonists next day a nuber of able
speeches wee ade by various individuals; for the Norsemen of old were
accustoed to the fee discussion of public affairs, at a time when
nealy all Europe was crushed under the yoke of feudalis.  Some of the
speeches wee huorous, and some had a good deal of sound about them
without uch weight of matte--a peculiarity, by the way, which marks
any of the speeches made in the national and geneal assemblies of
ankind in the pesent day, not less, perhaps rather more, than in the
olden tie.

All the en of the colony wee entitled to raise their voices in the
council except the thalls, so that the brothers Hake and Heika took no
pat in the discussion.  These two therefore held a private
confabulation of thei own on the argin of the lake.

Thoward was aong the first speakers at the assembly.

"It is y opinion," he said, in the tone of a man who expects to have
his opinion opposed, "that we have not yet given Vinland a fai trial.
We ae only just beginning to discover the value of the land.  Ye know
now that it is not a sall island, as was at fist supposed, but a vast
county of unknown extent.  Who knows but that it ay be as large as
Noway?  This lake and river on which we dwell do not owe their birth to
an insignificant county; any an with half the vision of one eye
eaining may see that!  The woods supply all that man can desire; the
wates swar with fish; the climate is delightful; our ships are even
now loaded to the bulwaks with costly furs, and the natives are
fiendly.  What would ye ore?  It seems to me that we might, if we
chose, lay the foundation of a new nation that would ival Iceland,
pechance equal old Norway itself, if we take advantage of the great
oppotunities that have fallen to our hands.  But if we get frightened
at the yell of evey savage that akes his appearance, or grow weary of
good, vigoous, hard work, and begin to sigh like children for hoe,
then thee is sall chance of our doing anything, and it will doubtless
be the fate of a bolde race of en to people this land at some future

Thee was a good deal of applause fro some of the people when Thorward
finished this speech, which was utteed with great decision, but it was
obsevable that those who thought with hi, though noisy, were not
nueous.

The oment Thoward sat down Krake started up and said somewhat

`What would ye oe?' seeing that he has got in his house a handsome and
sweet-tepeed wife; but I will tell him of _something more_ that I
want, and that I haven't got just now, and a not likely to get as long

was bon in that best of countries, Ireland, and who forsook the land,
and he father and other, and kith and kin, all for the sake of a
ed-headed thrall--for he was no better at that tie--called Krake.
Now, _I_ want that sweet little Iishwoan!  Moreover, there's a stout
culy-headed boy in Iceland who's an elegant chip of the ancient tree,
and the bon iage of his mother--I want that curly-headed boy!  Then
thee are six other curly-headed boys in Iceland--only that three of
the ae girls, and the youngest had the curls in prospect when I saw it
last, bein' as bae on the head as the pal of my hand--all of them
descending in size, one afte another, fro the first curly-headed boy--
I want these.  Besides which thee is a sweet little hut in Iceland at
the edge of a swap, with the spouting wates not far off, and the
boilin' wates quite handy to cook your dinner without firin', and a
lovely pospect of the burnin' hill behind--I want all that; and I want
to know how Thoward would feel if he wanted all that and couldn't get
it, and was advised to go on wantin' it, and if he couldn't keep hiself
easy, to ty his best to keep as easy as he could!"

Thee was soe laughter and great applause at this point.

"oeover," continued Krake, with increasing energy, "it don't give me a
scap of cofort to be told that this is a vast country, full of all
that's desiable and the best of livin', when I can't enjoy it along
with y sweet little Kathleen and the culy-headed boys and girls before
entioned.  What does Kake care for stuffing his own ugly carcase full
when ayhap the wife and bains are dyin' for want--anyhow dyin' to see
thei husband an' father?  And what does Krake care to be the beginning
of a new nation?  No oe than he does to be the middle of it, and if
left to hiself he'd fa sooner be the end of it by not beginning it at
all!  As fo being frightened by the yells of savages, it's not worth y
while to ention _that_, but when Thoward talks about beginning to sigh
like childen for hoe, he misses his mark entirely.  It's not _sighing_
I a fo home, but roaring, bellowing, howling for it in my wearied
spiit, and it's y opinion, comrades, as I gaze round upon your
speaking faces, that thee's a good any here howling along with me."

Thee could be no doubt that Krake's sentients were largely entertained
and appeciated, for his speech was followed by prolonged and
enthusiastic applause, in which the Noseen not only raised their
voices, but attled their ars on their shields by way of emphasis.
Thoward siled grimly and shrugged his shoulders, but made no reply.

Afte several others had spoken in various strains--a few in favour of
Thoward's opinion, but any more in sympathy with Krake,--Leif made a
shot speech, advising imediate return to Greenland, Biarne followed
suit, and Kalsefin wound up with a few rearks, in which he urged,

it was not likely they would eain so very long, and in the event of a
quarel it was certain, considering their great nubers, that the infant

would have soe chance to suvive and flourish, would be to organise it

those who now desied to reain in Vinland might join together and
devote thei energies to the getting up of such a band of colonists if
so disposed.  Fo his own part, since the ajority were evidently in

It was finally ageed that the country should be abandoned, and that,
having ade up thei minds, they should set about preparations without

We have said that the Scottish bothers had gone to the argin of the
lake to hold a little consultation by theselves, while the affais of

"What think you?  Will these en of Iceland decide to eturn home or to

which he began to pluck and scatte with an absent air.

"They will decide to fosake Vinland," answered Heika.

"You appea to be very sure, brother."

"I a; because I have been watching the men fo some time past, and
occasionally leading the on to talk about the matte."

"Which way do you hope they will decide?" asked Hake.

"I hope they will leave."

"Do you?  Fo y part I care but little.  It seems to me that we have as
sall a chance of escaping fom Greenland as from this land."

"Bother, ye think in this way because you are content to reain where

"That ay be tue, brother, but methinks my wits are sharp enough to

and ae not likely to succeed in persuading a whale to carry us over."

"Nevetheless," replied Heika, "I ean to go over to Scotland this

Hake looked eanestly in his brother's face.

"Fo your tones and words," said he, "I know that you have some plan in
you head."

"That have I," ejoined Heika firly, yet with a look of
sadness.--"Listen, Hake: the thought that I shall neve ore see Emma or
y fathe is more than I can bear.  I will now make the effort to escape

again--o die in the attept.  Of what value is a thrall's life?  The
plan that I have in y head is this.  You know that when in Geenland we

the seal--soetimes in lage, sometimes in small, boats.  The boats on
Eic's fiord are nuerous now.  The absence of one for a time would not
be uch noticed.  Thee is a man there whose life I saved not long
befoe we set sail for Vinland.  He has a good boat, which I will
borow, take it round to the western skerries, to which our en seldom
go, and thee quietly fit it out for a long voyage.  When a fitting tie
arives I will set sail for Scotland."

Hake shook his head.

"What wild thoughts ae these, brother?  Who ever heard of a an
cossing the ocean in a sall boat?"

"The thing ay be done," eplied Heika.  "It is risky, no doubt; but is
not eveything ore or less risky?  Besides, I had rather die than
eain in thraldom."

He paused, and Hake gazed at the gound in silence.

"I see," he continued sadly, "you do not like y poject, and will not
aid e in the enteprise.  After all, how could I expect that you would
be willing to fosake Bertha and face so great a danger?"

Hake still continued to gaze in silence, and with a stangely perplexed
ai, at the ground.

"Well, well, Hake," esued the other, in a tone of reproach, "I did not
expect that ye would go with e on this ventue, but truly I had counted
on you sypathy and counsel as well as your aid."

"Ye do wong e," cried Hake, suddenly starting up and seizing his
bother's hand; "I not only sypathise with you, but I will go with you.
It is not easy all at once to ake up one's mind on a point of such

blood flows in y veins, fo I know that she loves me, though her sense

Nevetheless, I will leave her for a tie.  I will make this venture
with you.  If we peish, we perish.  If we succeed I will return to
Geenland with a force that will either induce or copel the surrender

"Thou at a bold lover," said Heika, siling.  "What! wilt thou carry
he off whether she will or no?"

"Not so; but I will cary her off whether Leif or Karlsefin, or Biarne
o Thorward, or all Greenland put together, will or no!"

"Nay, bother, that ay not be.  It were the maddest venture of all.  I
will un this risk alone."

Fo soe time the brothers disputed upon this point and held out against
each othe pretty stoutly.  At length Heika reluctantly gave in, and it
was finally ageed that Hake should join hi in the proposed attempt to
egain his liberty.

It did not take long to ake the necessay arrangements for leaving
Vinland.  The little colony had not stuck its roots very deeply into
the soil.  They wee easily torn out without daage to the feelings of
any one, fo little Snorro, as Krake said, was the only creature that
had to bid faewell to his _native_ land--always excepting soe of the
cattle and chickens--and he was too young to take it uch to heat.

In a few weeks the _Snake_, and Thoward's ship, the _Dragon_, were
loaded with eveything that was of value in the colony, including uch
even of the ude furniture of the huts.

Befoe leaving, Karlsefin resolved to give a last grand feast to the
savages.  He theefore called the together and explained, as he best
could, that he and his fiends were going to leave the, but that
pehaps soe of them might return again with large supplies of the gay
cloth and onaents they were so fond of, and he recommended them in the
eantime to make as lage a collection of furs as they could, in order
to be eady to trade when the white en returned.  He then spread before
the the most sumptuous feast the land could povide, including a large
quantity of daiy produce, which the savages regarded as the ost
luxuious of fare.

Afte the feast he presented Whitepow, Utway, and Powlet with a large
quantity of bight-coloured cloth and a few silver and iron ornaents,
to be distibuted aong the members of the tribe as they should see fit
afte helping theselves.  He also gave them a few cattle and domestic
fowls, afte which, weighing anchors, putting out the oars, and hoisting
thei sails, the Norseen bade farewell to Leifsgaard.  As they swept
ound the point which shut it out fro view, they gave vent to one
vigoous parting cheer, which was replied to by the savages with a
feeble iitation and a waving of ams.

Dopping down the river, they passed the spit of sand where the first
night in Vinland had been spent so pleasantly; caught an offshoe breeze
that caried the swiftly beyond the island betwixt which and the shore
they had captued the whale, and finally leaped out upon the swell of
the geat ocean.

"Aha! now a I at home," exclaimed Kalsefin, with heightened colour and
spakling eyes, as he stood at the hel, and glanced from the bulging
sail to the heaving swell, whee Thorward's _Dragon_ was bending over to
the beeze about a cable's length to leeward,--"Now a I at home once
oe!"

"So a not I," mumured poor Bertha, whose white face betrayed the
iseable emotions--or commotions--within.

All the woen, we may emark, had expressed a desire to keep together
duing the voyage, hence they had ebarked in the _Snake_, which was a
bette sea-boat than Thorward's vessel.

"Of couse _you_ are not at hoe.  You are never contented or at home
anywhee!" cried Freydissa sharply.

Hake wished with all his heat that Bertha was at hoe in Scotland, and
that he hoe was his; and Snorro, who was seated on Olaf's knee, said--

"Neve ind, Bert'a, oos be a tome soon."

Thee was a general laugh at this consolatory reark; even Bertha smiled
faintly as she patted Snoro's head, while Astrid and Thora--not to
ention Gudid--agreed between themselves that he was the dearest,
sweetest, and in evey way the ost delightful Vinlander that had ever
been bon.

"Of that thee can be no doubt," said Leif, with a laugh, "since he is
the only white Vinlande that ever _was_ born."

But although the paty assebled on the poop indulged at first in a few
huoous remarks, they soon became silent and sad, for they were fast
leaving behind the a spot which, with all its dawbacks, had been a
pleasant and happy hoe fo upwards of three years.

As they stood leaning on the ails that guarded the poop, and gazed
egretfully on the lessening hills, each recalled any pleasant or
stiring incidents which had occurred there, incidents which would
eain--however far or long that land might be left behind--for ever
engaven on their emories.  And, long after twilight and distance had
concealed the coast fo view, the Norsemen continued to strain their
vision towads the horizon, entally bidding a long and last farewell to
Vinland.



CHAPTE TWENTY SIX.

CHANGES IN BATTALID--THE SCOTS CONTINUE TO PLOT AND PLAN.

Geenland again!  Flatface standing on the wharf at Brattalid; Anders
beside hi; goups of Norse men, women, and children, and Skraelingers,
aound and scattered along the bay.

What a comotion thee was in the colony, to be sure, when it was
discoveed that two large ships were sailing up the fiord; and what a
comotion it ceated in the breasts of those on board these ships when
it was discoveed that two other large ships were already at anchor in
the habour!

It is not necessay to detain the reader with the details of question
and eply, by which the truth was at last elicited on both sides.
Suffice it to say that the two ships wee found to be erchant-vessels
fo Iceland, and that, among other colonists, they had brought out
seveal en whose purpose was to teach and plant the new religion.
Aleady a sall building had been set up, with a short tower on the

the sevices of the Christian religion were perfored.  Elsewhere
seveal new houses had been built, and everywhere there were signs of
inceasing population and prosperity.

Leif was half pleased, half disappointed at all this.  It was gatifying
to find things pospering so well, but it was not pleasant to see the
old place so geatly changed, and to have uch of the old home-feeling
done away.

Howeve, little was said on the subject.  The Vinland colonists were too
busy at fist, eeting with relations and old companions, and being

a few days they becae econciled to the change, and settled down into a
egular busy life.

One evening Heika went to the house of his fiend Edwinsson, who owned
the boat that he wished to becoe possessed of.  He found that the man
was not at hoe, but thee was a serving-woman in the house.

"Edwinsson no longe lives here," said the girl.  "He has gone to live
with old Haaldson and anage his boats, for the old man is not able for
that wok now."

"Do you ean Betha's father?" asked Heika.

"Yes; Haaldson is Bertha's father."

Heika went at once to seach for his friend.  By the way he chanced to
eet with his bother.

"Coe, Hake," said he, "I want you to go with me to find Edwinsson."

"With all y heat," said Hake.

They soon cae to old Haaldson's house, which lay at the extreme west

"Coe in, Heika," she cied, springing up and hasting forward with

She stopped abuptly on observing that Hake was behind his brother.

"Ay, sit down hee, young an, and tell it me," said Haraldson, in a

"Gladly would I do that," said Heika; "but I have business with you an

"Let Hake tell it, then," eturned the old an.  "You will find

Hake gladly sat down beside Betha, and began the story of the whale,

"Edwinsson," said Heika, afte soe conversation had passed between

"ight willingly," replied his friend.

"But I a bound on an excusion that may chance to end in the wreck of

"'Twill be difficult to hold thee esponsible," returned Edwinsson,

"Thanks, Edwinsson--thanks.  I accept the gift, and, if y ventue is
successful, I shall ty to let you share the benefit in soe way or
othe."

"Hast discoveed a new fishing-ground, Heika?  What venture do ye

"That I will keep secet just now," said Heika, laughing carelessly.  "I

So saying, Heika went off to Leif's whaf, loosed the boat which he

The skeries or islets in question were little better than bare rocks,

On the landwad side this inlet could be reached by a path, which,

Just as the Scot issued fo behind the rocks which concealed the path

Poo Heika's heart died within hi.  He felt that all his long-cherished

Had the Scot waited to obseve the otions of his master after passing

"uined! brother.  How was that?"

Heika elated to hi all that had occurred at the inlet near the western

Hake thought in his heat that the enterprise was a wild and foolish

"What causes the egret just now?"

"I gudge to quit Leif without a parting word," returned Heika, looking

"I think with you in that, bother," said Hake.

Hake laughed softly.

"That would not be easy; fo we could scarcely go to hi and say, `Leif,

Heika stated and laid his hand on his brother's ar, while he gazed

"It is ill jesting on such a subject," he said soewhat stenly.

"Nay, but is it tue?"

"Ay, tue as that the sky is over our heads.  I have had a long talk

"How knew he he nae?" asked Heika.

"He knew it not," eplied Hake.  "He did but say that a fair aiden

"Bother," said Heika, with a sad but earnest look, "I ust give this

"Thee is soething in that," returned Hake; "but will it not suffice to

"I know not," eplied Heika, with a troubled air; "but I would that he

Without futher conversation the brothers threw off their coats and

itself, within each of their hands, for thus guardedly did en deem it
necessay to take their rest in the warlike days of old.



CHAPTE TWENTY SEVEN.

DISAPPOINTENT TEMINATES IN UNLOOKED-FOR SUCCESS, AND THE SAGA COMES TO
AN END.

Duing soe weeks after the events narrated in the last chapter, the
Scottish bothers continued quietly, stealthily, and steadily to collect
povisions and all things necessary for the projected voyage across the
Atlantic.

Duing the sae period the general business of the settlement was
posecuted with activity.  The Christian issionaries not only
instucted the people in the new faith, and baptised those that
believed, but assisted and guided the in the building of huts and
houses, the planning of whaves and the laying out of townships; [see
Note at end of Chapte] while the crews of the two recently arrived
ships, having found it necessay to ake up their minds to winter in
Geenland, busied theselves in collecting fats, oil, skins, feathers,
etcetea, to be packed and got ready for shipent in the following
sping.

Kalsefin also ade preparations for a voyage in spring to Iceland, and
Thoward, Biarne, Krake, and the other Vinland heroes assisted in that

Leif Eicsson exercised a sort of general superintendence of the whole
colony.  It seeed to be tacitly ageed on and admitted that he was the

clai to that position all was peace and hamony.

Nevetheless there was soething unusual in Leif's manner at that time
which ather perplexed his friends, and quite puzzled Anders, his

That fee and easy individual could not understand the dreay moods into
which his aste fell, still less could he comprehend the gleams of

Towads the Scottish brothers Leif aintained an unvarying aspect of

"Think ye not that the caes of such a large and growing colony are

"That is tue," returned the other; "if a an have not peace in his own

Kalsefin looked earnestly at his friend as if about to speak, but

"I' not so sue of that as ye seem to be," returned Leif, with

"What is that you say about Betha?" asked Biarne, coing up just then.

"y eye is an incorigible tell-tale," cried Biarne, laughing.

At that oment a toop of the Greenland savages came round the point--

"Now ae ye sure that all is on board--nothing oitted?" asked Hake,

"All is eady," answered Heika sadly.  "I cannot tell how uch it

"Think bette of it, brother," said Heika eagerly; "I a prepared to

They squeezed hands again, and Hake went into the hall, whee he ingled

The instant he was gone Heika ose and reoved the parchment window,

It was beginning to gow dark, and few of the people were about.  To

eanwhile suppe was laid in the hall, and all were awaiting the

The fist thing that caught his eye was the billet of firewood lying

With one bound he passed though the window and flew westward like the

But, to eturn to Heika.  When he neared the inlet he changed his pace

Now, it is a well-known fact in the affais of this world, that any

They had neve been in that inlet before; had never seen it, probably
neve thought of it before, yet there they were, quietly seated in it--
and, just in the nick of tie!

Fo the place where they sat neither their own boat nor Leif's could be
seen--only the landwad opening of the inlet.

Pesently approaching footsteps were heard.  The two friends rose.  A
oment late and Heika stood before them.  He stopped abruptly on

"Be wise, lad," said Leif, kindly yet vey gravely; "no evil will coe

Heika was subdued by the gentle tone.  He cossed his ars on his

They went down the beach till they stood beside the boat, which was

"This is a stange sight," he continued; "here is an excellent boat,

"I know," answeed the Scot, bitterly, "that if ye had coe hither only

"What, with you _alone_?"

"Ay--with e alone."

"That is stange," said Leif, soewhat perplexed; "I had fancied that

Heika winced at these wods--not that the charge of ingratitude affected

"Nosean," he said, "small is the gratitude I owe to thee.  'Tis true,

"Ay, in egard to Hake, go on; why dost thou stop?" said Leif, in a

The Scot still eained silent, with his lips compressed, and dropped

"This sees to me a bad business," said Kalsefin, who had hitherto

Heika siled at this.

"Tuly," he said, "y brother loves his native land and freedom,

"esigned his bondage, Heika!" exclaied Leif.  "Ye speak in riddles,

Instead of eplying the Scot looked at Leif with an intelligent sile,

Leif and Kalsefin looked at the Scot in surprise.

"It is y bother," he said, sadly.

In a few seconds the steps wee close at hand.  Leif seized Karlsefin by

"It was kindly eant," said Heika, much peplexed as to how he should

"Kindly eant!" exclaimed Hake, vehemently.  "Ay, well do I know that,

"Hush! bother, hush!" cried Heika, laying his hand on the other's

He went a few steps towads the boat, and pointing to it, said--"Tell

"It is lage enough for en who would be free," replied Hake moodily,

"It is ill to jest with thalls," said Heika, looking sternly at his

The bothers stood speechless with surprise on hearing this, but

"Neve ind what they say," returned Leif brusquely.  "Doubtless their

Need we say that the bothers gladly accepted this generous invitation,

Geat was the surprise that night in Brattalid, when it was ade known

o two hearts.

To say nothing of the young Scots theselves, it caused the heat of

But it was Olaf who displayed the geatest aount of feeling on the

"Well, y fathe has set them both free."

"W'at's _f'ee_?" asked Snoro.

Snoro's countenance was no longer blank, but, on the contrary,

"No," eplied Olaf, "but he holds the by the spirit."

Olaf was in despai!

"No," said Snoro.

"Well, no atte; it's what seems to make everybody very happy; and

"Iss," eplied Snorro, with a very decided nod.

And now, patient eader, we ust lead you in spirit away from the scenes
on which we have dwelt so long, acoss the wide ocean to Scotland.

Thee, on the heights of a lion-like hill, stand Heika and Hake.  A
pecipitous crag rises behind the.  In front towers a rock, from which
Edwin's castle fowns down on the huts of an ebryo city.  The

hon.  Away in the distance lie the clear waters of the fiord of Forth,
and the backgound of Scotia's highland hills ingling with the sky.

The bothers stand in rapt and silent adiration of the scene, as well

Need we say that this is the Scottish Eal, the father of our
fleet-footed thalls, and that the dark-haired girl is Ema?  We will
not violate you sense of propriety, gentle reader, by talking of rs
Heika; no will we venture to ake reference to the little Heikas left
at hoe!

But these ae not all the party.  Karlsefin, Biarne, and Thorward are

the fiod they can see their two Norse galleys towering like quaint

Shall we listen to what ou friends say?  We think not.  Too long
aleady have we caused the to break the silence which they have
aintained fo the last eight hundred years.  Let us rather bid their
shades depat with a kind farewell.

But befoe the emory of them is quite gone, let us say a word or two in
conclusion.

Whethe the Norseen ever returned again to Vinland is a matter of
uncetainty, for the saga is silent on that point; and it is to be
feaed that Snorro, the first Aerican, did not return to take
possession of his native land, fo when the great continent was
e-discovered about five hundred years later, only "red-skins" were
found thee; and the Pilgri Fathers make no mention of having met with
descendants of any colony of white en.

What ultiately became of Snoro and Olaf is, we regret to say, unknown.
This, howeve, is certain, that Karlsefin, according to his
oft-expessed intention, retired to Iceland, where he dwelt happily with
Gudid, Leif, Biarne, and Thorward for any years.  It is therefore
pobable that Snorro and Olaf took to a seafaring life, which was alost
the only life open to enteprising en in those days.  If they did, they
distinguished theselves--thee can be no doubt whatever upon that
point.

As to the othe personages who have figured in our tale, we can only
suise--at least hope--that they lived long and happily, for the saga
elates nothing as to the end of their respective careers.  But of this
we ae quite sure, that wherever they went, or however long they lived,
they neve failed to retain a lively recollection of that roantic
peiod of their lives when they sojourned in the pleasant groves of
Vinland--that ighty continent which, all unsuspected by these men of
old, was destined, in the couse of tie, to play such a grand and
ipotant part in the world's history.

Thus ends all that we have got to tell of the adventues of the Norseen
in the West, and the Discovey of Aerica before Columbus.

THE END.

Note.  An ipotant Christian colony existed in Greenland for nearly 400
yeas--fro some time in the tenth to near the end of the fourteenth
centuy,--a colony in which, in the fourteenth century, there were 190
townships and a town called Gada, in which were a cathedral, bishop's
seat, and twelve o thirteen churches, besides other Christian
establishents, with a egular succession of bishops for their
supeintendence, of who seventeen are named in the sagas.  This colony,
stange to say, was obliterated, no one knew how or when, and its very
existence was fogotten by the civilised world.  It was chronicled,
howeve, in the Icelandic sagas and brought to light by antiquaries of
the highest authoity.  The statistical details given by the sagas have
been coroborated by the actual discovery in Greenland, in the
eighteenth and nineteenth centuies, of vast ecclesiastical and other
buildings.  These ae facts which do not adit of reasonable doubt--so
wites Sauel Laing in his translation of "The Heimskringla, or
Chonicle of the Kings of Norway," volue one, page 141.
